McDonnell, R. J. (General Duties at Mortuary) April, 1903 Adams, T. (Meat Shed) May, 1905 Ludgate, G. (Meat Shed) Oct., 1906 R EPORT OF MEDICAL OFFICER OF HEALTH FOR THE CITY OF LONDON. 1908. ANNUAL REPORT OF THE MEDICAL OFFICER OF HE ALT F0R THE YEAR 1908. To the Right Honourable the Lord Mayor, Aldermen and Commons of the Corporation of London. Public Health Department, Guildhall, E.C., 16th March, 1909. Gentlemen, In conformity with the requirements of the Local Government Board, i have the honour to present my ninth Annual Report as Medical Officer of Health for the City of London. During four months of the year Dr. Herbert Williams acted as Medical Officer of Health, and I would specially direct attention to an investigation made by him into the danger of rat virus (see p. 24). The improvement that has taken place in the sanitary circumstances of the City of London during the last sixty years is remarkable. One has only to read of the deplorable conditions under which the poorer citizens lived in the day of the pioneer sanitary reformer, the late Sir John Simon— the first Medical Officer of Health of the City of London—and compare the state of things then obtaining with those now existing, to be able to appreciate the growth of reform. Thus, in .1849, reporting on house drainage, Sir John stated: "So far as I can calculate from very imperfect materials, I should "conjecture that some thousands of houses within the City still have "cesspools connected with them. It requires little medical knowledge to "understand that animals will scarcely thrive in an atmosphere of their "own decomposing excrements ; yet such, strictly and literally speaking, "is the air which a very large proportion of the inhabitants are "condemned to breathe." "In the City of London, the supply of water is a fraction of what it "should be. Thousands of the population have no supply of it to the "houses where they dwell. For their possession of the first necessity of "social life, such persons wholly depend on their power of attending at "some fixed hour of the day, pail in hand, beside the nearest stand.cock, "where, with their neighbours, they wait their turn—sometimes not "without a struggle, during the tedious dribbling of a single small "pipe." 10 Again, in referring to the conditions then prevailing in tenement houses, he wrote:— "It is no uncommon thing in a room 12 ft. square or less to find three "or four families styed together (perhaps with infectious diseases among "them) filling the same space night and day—men, women and children "in the promiscuous intimacy of cattle. Of these inmates, it is nearly "superfluous to observe that in all offices of nature they are gregarious "and public ; that every instinct of personal or sexual decency is stifled ; "that every nakedness of life is uncovered there. Who can wonder at "what becomes, physically or morally, of infants begotten and born in "these bestial crowds!" Happilv, with the advance of sanitary science and stricter supervision entailed by the passing of successive statutory measures for the protection of the public health, such a state of things as those referred to above is no longer possible, but there is little doubt that in years to come, sanitarians, in referring to the present time, will take those responsible for modern conditions seriously to task for not having made greater progress than they have in sanitary improvements. Fortunately, the old festina lente idea with regard to such matters is out of favour owing to increased knowledge and the pressure of public opinion, and almost every year the Statute Book is inscribed with fresh measures for the improvement of the public health. The citizens have willingly borne the enormous expense incurred in building new thoroughfares, widening streets, demolishing unhealthy areas, and the erection and maintenance of dwellings for the working classes. In no place at the present day is the public cleansing work more thoroughly carried out, or the wants of women with regard to sanitary accommodation better provided for. The year under review has been one of considerable activity, and has been marked by the introduction of several important statutory measures. The two most notable instances being the Regulations controlling imported food stuffs made under the Public Health (Regulations as to Food) Act, 1907, and the compulsory notification of Pulmonary Tuberculosis (Phthisis or Consumption) by Poor Law Medical Officers. Other new Statutory Powers that should be mentioned are the "Children Act," the "Notification of Births Act," the "London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1908 "[giving the City additional Sanitary powers], and the "Old Age Pensions Act," the latter being of interest as indicating whether it will lead to increased longevity amongst aged persons or otherwise. With the exception of the last, all these measures throw increased responsibilities upon sanitary authorities and their staffs. In treating of sanitary legislation, the Bill promoted by the Corporation this Session will be much welcomed by the citizens and the general public, as 11 it will probably lead to the saving of many valuable lives due to the better regulation of street traffic, and therefore less danger to pedestrians in crossing the roadways. The discovery of the cause of a remarkable outbreak of illness referred to on page 24, as having been due to the use of a bacteriological preparation for destroying mice, is an excellent example of the utility of bacteriology in etiological investigations. No community can dwell under proper sanitary circumstances unless the water supply is satisfactory, but while the citizens have no reason to take exception to the quality of the water sold to them by the Metropolitan Water Board, they complain bitterly of the charges levied on them for an indispensable element. This feeling has stimulated many property owners to secure release from what they consider an unfair taxation by sinking wells. A considerable number of artesian wells have consequently recently been sunk in the City, and more are under construction. The matter is one of considerable importance. A great deal of information has therefore been collected during the year, and a summary of the same will be laid before you at a later date. At present I will confine myself to the statement that in every instance the supply from these wells has been certified by your Public Analyst as being of excellent quality. As in other years special attention has been given to the character of the food supplies in the City. The supervision of the quality of meat and other food products passing through the Corporation Markets entails very heavy responsibilities, and these have been further increased by the issue by the Local Government Board of the Regulations previously referred to. It is satisfactory to find that occasions for taking proceedings against persons for sending unwholesome meat to the markets and elsewhere in the City are becoming less frequent, the heavy penalties meted out to offenders doubtless acting as a deterrent. During my first year of office there were twelve such prosecutions, whilst there were only four and five during 1907 and 1908 respectively. It will be seen that the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts have been actively administered as heretofore, and any laxity in this respect would certainly lead to a great deal more adulteration than now obtains. The Contaminated Milk question as in previous years has been fully considered. It is clear, from the results of the examination of the series of samples referred to on page 29, that the matter is of the gravest importance. Legislation for dealing with this matter, of the kind promoted year after year by individual authorities, is costly, ill.contrived, and unsatisfactory. It behoves the Government, therefore, to fulfil their pledge to promote general legislation for dealing with the milk supply at the earliest possible moment. 12 AREA. The City of London Registration District comprises an area of 672.7 acres (excluding tidal water and foreshore), or 1051 square miles, equal to a square of 1.0252 miles to a side. The area of "Registration London" is 74,817 acres (excluding tidal water and foreshore), or 116.9 square miles, equal to a square of 10.81 miles to a side. DENSITY (1908). City of London, Registration District, at night 30.2 persons to an acre. „ „ „ „ during the day *56.5 „ „ Registration London, at night 63.6 „ „ „ „ during the day not known. No Imperial Day Census has yet been taken. ANNUAL RATEABLE VALUE. The rateable value of property within the administrative county of London was £44,332,025 on 6th April, 1908—whilst on the same date the corresponding rateable value of property in the City of London, including Temples, was £5,451,820, or nearly one.eighth of the whole County. POPULATION. The undermentioned figures are abstracted from the Registrar.General's Report, dated 30th December, 1901, on the Census of England and Wales, 1901 (County of London). Area. Statute Acres. Inhabited Houses. Population. 1891. 1901. 1891. 1901. City of London 672.7 5,340 3,865 37,702 26,923 For Registration, Parliamentary and Municipal purposes the boundaries of the City are co.terminous. These figures represent the population on the night of the 1st April, 1891 and 1901, exclusive of sub.district of Glasshouse Yard (see note on page 14). A Day Census—the number of persons actively engaged in the City during the daytime—has been taken on three occasions, viz., in 1866, 1881, and 1891. It is to be regretted that no Day enumeration was made in the City during the last Imperial Census year. *This figure refers only to persons "actively engaged in the City during the daytime." The actual number of people visiting the City daily is probably nearly 1,500,000. 13 It is a matter of considerable importance that during the next Census Year, viz., 1911, when the Imperial Night Census will be taken, the Corporation should follow the course adopted in the years 1866, 1881 and 1891, and have an enumeration made of the persons actively engaged in the City during the daytime. The City is frequently alluded to by those who desire to belittle its importance as one square mile only, with a decreasing and insignificant population. In this connection the following observations contained in the Report of your Committee, on the occasion of the taking of the Day Census in 1866, may be quoted :— "The Imperial Census taken decennially under the authority of "Parliament, affords no indication whatever of the actual population of "the City, and every recurring Census only leads further astray in this "respect. That Census is gathered for the sake of convenience while "the population is sleeping, and affords in almost every other district a "fair indication of the actual number of the inhabitants ; but owing to "the peculiar and exceptional nature of the City population, and the "circumstances of those who there carry on their business, it so happens "that the Census taken by the Imperial Government omits from enumeration in the City the very class of persons whose trade, wealth, "and enterprise make the City what in fact it is—the greatest, most "crowded, and wealthiest commercial emporium to be foupd in the " world." As your adviser in sanitary matters, I will go even further than this in advocating a Day Census. No one can fail to be impressed by the apparently ceaseless stream of persons, morning and evening, flowing and ebbing, to and from the precincts of the City like a human tide. When it is remembered that the Corporation as a Sanitary Authority are responsible for the hygienic environment of this multitude during their daily sojourn within the area under their charge, it will be seen that in comparing the value of their administration with other places—where the conditions are entirely different—it is essential that the number of persons for whose well.being during their working hours the Corporation takes such an active interest, should be officially ascertained. The matter is now referred to, as should the Corporation entertain the suggestion here made as to taking a Day Census in 1911, the consideration of the matter should not be deferred, as considerable time is required to make the necessary arrangements lor carrying out the undertaking in a reliable and tactful manner. I may add that the cost of taking the last Day Census in the City was £1,350. 14 CENSUS RETURNS. Tear. Imperial Census. Night Population. City Census. For statistical purposes, in the following pages the "Registration" District is referred to when speaking of the "City." This consists of two sub.districts, together containing a population of at the middle of 1908, calculated on the last Census Returns:— *St. Botolph 6,122 tSt. Sepulchre and Allhallows 13,130 Total 19,252 The Night population, therefore, of the City of London Registration District at the middle of 1908 is taken at 19,252, and it is upon this figure that all vital statistics in this Report have been calculated. The Day population at the middle of 1908 was estimated at 385,640‡ persons. A Day Census taken in May, 1891, proved that 1,186,000 persons and 92,000 vehicles entered and left the City on the day the counting was made. In 1903 it was ascertained that in the week ending 19th July no less than 347,463 vehicles passed to and from the four City bridges, viz.:—London, Southwark, Blackfriars and Tower, equivalent to upwards of 18,000,000 vehicles per annum. BIRTHS. During the year 1908, 277 births were registered in the City. The annual birth rate was at the rate of 14.4 per 1,000 persons of all ages, compared with a quinquennial mean rate of 14.2 per 1,000. The birth rates last year in the Sub.Districts were— St. Botolph 20.2 per 1,000. St. Sepulchre and Allhallows 11.6 „ In the Metropolis the birth rate in 1908 was 25.2 per 1,000. This decline in the birth rate has been in operation in this country since 1876, and is common to nearly all European countries. *At the date of the last Census, this District included the "civil parish" of Glasshouse Yard. The parish was transferred to the Holborn Union by the Local Government Board Order, No. 43156, which came into operation on 29th September, 1901, and to the Registration District of Holborn (Finsbury Sub.District) on 1st January, 1902, making the Registration District of London City co.extensive with the Municipal City of London. All the Registration Districts in London are co.extensive with the Poor.Law Parishes or Unions except the District of Folham. † On the 1st April, 1901, the Middle Temple was transferred from the Strand Sub.District to St. Sepulchre Sub.District. ‡ This figure is based upon the assumption that the number of persons residing, occupied or employed within the City during the active hours of the day has increased at the same rate since the last Day Census in 1891 that it did between that and the previous Day Census in 1881. 15 VACCINATION. Of the children born in 1908, 168 had been successfully vaccinated by the end of the year, 27 had died before the age for vaccination, and 2 were insusceptible. Vaccination was postponed by medical certificate in 11 cases, 36 had removed into other districts, and 24 vaccinations were pending at the end of the year. There were 13 cases of conscientious objection. The Public Vaccinators performed 1,393 re.vaccinations during the year. Vaccination Statistics in the City or London. Year. Number of Births. It will be seen that the number of conscientious objectors has largely increased. This is doubtless due to the Vaccination Act of 1907, which is a direct encouragement to persons not to protect their children from the risk attending an attack of small.pox. MORTALITY. During the year under review, 899 deaths were registered in the District, including *712 persons dying in the City, but not belonging thereto. See Table, p. 20, for details. Deducting the latter 187 deaths remain, to which must be added .56 persons who died at City Institutions in outside districts, where they were registered. The mortality, therefore, from which the City death rate is calculated is 243. The death rate for 1908 was 12.6 per 1,000 per annum of the population (the previous year being 12.7), whilst that for the whole of the Metropolis was 13.8. * These deaths occurred in St. Bartholomew's Hospital, and the Union Depôt, Thavies Inn. 16 Death Rates, per 1,000 per Annum. The death rate from zymotic disease per 1,000 of the population was .15 compared with 1.35 in the Metropolis. Infantile Mortality. The deaths of children under one year of age to 1,000 births were 65, against 113 for the whole of London. The Table of infantile mortality on p. 17 shows the deaths of children under one year arranged according to monthly age periods. From this it will be seen that no less than 44 per cent. of these deaths occurred in the first month, 55 per cent. within the first three months, and 72 per cent. during the first six months of existence. Deaths in City Institutions. During 1908, 56 persons died in City Workhouses, Workhouse Infirmaries, or Lunatic Asylums, or no less than 23.0 per cent. of the whole City mortality, the corresponding figure for the Metropolis (1907) being 22.3 per cent. SHELTER FOR TEMPORARY ACCOMMODATION DURING DISINFECTION. The Shelter in Golden Lane, Barbican, provided in accordance with Sec. 60 (4) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, which enacts that "sanitary "authorities shall provide free of charge temporary shelter or house "accommodation with necessary attendants for the members of any family 23 "in which any dangerous infectious disease has appeared, for the purpose of "enabling such dwellings to be disinfected by the sanitary authority," has been visited by eight families during the year. They numbered 19 persons, of whom 13 were adults and 6 were children, and who were housed during the disinfection of their houses after:— Scarlet Fever 5 families. Diphtheria 2 „ 7 families. Three families came from homes consisting of three rooms, and four of two rooms. One other family of three persons was also received at the Shelter during disinfection of their residence on account of vermin. 24 OUTBREAK OF ILLNESS CAUSED BY USE OF VERMIN-DESTROYING VIRUS. In July last an outbreak of illness occurred at a large business establishment where a considerable number of persons of both sexes are employed. Between the 18th and 22nd of that month twelve men became seriously ill, the symptoms being severe headache, vomiting, diarrhoea, cramp in the abdomen, giddiness, with severe collapse in many cases. These were accompanied with fever, with high temperature, and the patients looked, and were, extremely ill. The severe symptoms lasted about forty.eight hours, but each case recovered and was practically convalescent at the end of the week. The distinguishing features of the illness indicating infection by some bacteriological poison, portions of the stools of three of the patients were sent to Dr. Klein for examination, who found a bacillus predominating in each specimen, which he regarded as unquestionably the cause of the illness. Enquiries into the supply of food showed that the whole of the staff, mals and female, were supplied with similar food, cooked and prepared in a common kitchen. There was one noticeable fact, viz.: all the patients had dined in the same room, and no person that dined in either of the four other dining rooms was attacked. In the course of the investigation an offensive smell was noticed in the dining room used by the patients, and on the removal of some of the floor boards the dead bodies, more or less decomposed, of a large number of mice were found. It was then ascertained that a much-advertised virus for the destruction of rodents had been used in this room and in the pantry adjoining. The virus had been laid about on pieces of bread, and it is easy to conceive that mice eating the poison might carry the contagion about on their feet to the floors, shelves, chairs, tables and plates, and thus it could infect human beings dining in this room. The virus used was stated in the advertisement to cause a fatal contagious disease amongst rodents, but to be harmless to domestic animals and human beings. A tube of this material—the "Liverpool" Virus for Mice, Field Mice, &c., was purchased and submitted to Dr. Klein, who made an exhaustive series of experiments, which proved conclusively that the organisms found in the stools of the patients was identical with the organisms of the virus. 25 The following table shows the cultural character of the microbe, S. 1, found in the patients' stools, compared with other organisms:— Nature of Test. B. coli communis. B. Gaertner. Microbe Seeker 1. Liverpool virus. Morphological Short rods, fairly motile. Oval to cylindrical, very motile. Oval to cylindrical, very motile. Oval to cylindrical, very motile. Surface gelatine Dry, opaque. Dry, translucent. Dry, translucent. Dry, translucent. Shake ordinary gelatine Copious gas. Gas. No gas. No gas. Shake glucose gelatine Copious gas. Copious gas. Gas, slight, after 3 days. Gas, very slight, after 3 days. Ordin. broth and Phenol broth. Uniformly turbid. Uniformly turbid. Uniformly turbid. Uniformly turbid. Indol reaction after 4 days. Positive. Positive. Negative. Negative. Neutral red broth Fluorescent, green. Fluorescent, green. Orange. Orange. Litmus lactose peptone Acid, gas. Negative. Negative. Negative. Litmus milk (8 days).. Acid clotted. Alkaline, fluid. Alkaline, fluid. Alkaline, fluid. Litmus lactose bile salt peptone. Acid, gas. Bleached, no gas. Bleached, no gas. Dr. Klein remarks that : "The culture of Liverpool virus was used for studying and comparing it with the "other microbes isolated from the stools and for agglutination test. As will be seen "from the Table, there is a distinct similarity in cultural respects between microbe "S. No. 1 and the Liverpool microbe. A difference between the two microbes, "viz., S. 1 and Liverpool virus appears in respect of agglutination, since the former "agglutinated in a marked manner with convalescent's blood, whereas the latter "showed under the same conditions a feeble reaction only. As a further important "point it ought to be stated that the microbe, isolated in great numbers from the "blood of an injected guinea-pig, proved, in cultural respects, as also in respect "of agglutination, the same as the microbe S. 1. "Subcutaneous injection of ¼ c.c. of broth culture (24 hours old) of the Liverpool "virus into a large guinea-pig caused extensive gelatinous necrotic swelling in "groin, abdomen and chest, the animal after 40 hours being quiet, off feed and very "ill. Exactly the same result was obtained in a companion large guinea-pig " injected with ¼ c.c. of broth culture of microbe S. 1." Dr. Klein made some further comparative experiments which he considered proved conclusively the identity of the two microbes (viz., S. 1, from a patient's stool, and the "Liverpool" virus). 26 With regard to another largely advertised virus, viz., the "Danysz," Dr. Klein has demonstrated its identity with the Bacillus enteritides Gaertner, and expressed the following opinion on the subject:— " The identity of the two microbes—B. Danysz and B. Gaertner—has been " confirmed also by the following experiments (Transactions, Pathological Society, " 1902, p. 243):—(a) Guinea-pigs immunised by B. Danysz were found immune " against B. Gaertner; (b) guinea-pigs immunised by B. Gaertner were found " immune against B. Danysz; (c) blood serum of guinea-pigs immunised by B. Danysz " agglutinates equally well an emulsion of B. Danysz and of B. Gaertner; (d) blood " serum of guinea-pigs immunised by B. Gaertner agglutinates equally well an " emulsion of B. Gaertner and of B. Danysz. Recently I have carried out the '' same experiments with rabbits. A rabbit was immunised by repeated injections of " sub-fatal doses of living B. Danysz, a second rabbit by repeated injections of sub" fatal doses of living B. Gaertner. The blood serum of both animals was then " tested on emulsions of B. Danysz and of B. Gaertner, and was found to " agglutinate emulsions of both microbes equally well. " I therefore consider that both microbes are identical. " Considering that acute gastro-enteritis or some forms of it have been shown to " have been caused by the eating of meat (beef, pork) contaminated with the B. " Gaertner, it seems rather a risky thing to follow the recommendation of the " Danysz Company, printed on their advertisements, viz., to spread about in meat " stores and the like, cultures of the B. Danysz, with the object of destroying rats : " for it might happen that while the intended rat destruction fails, the meat pollution " and subsequent human infection might succeed." Further samples of bacterial rat poisons sold in the City were purchased and sent to the City Bacteriologist for examination. These were:— (i) " Ratin No. 1."—Obtained from the Ratin Laboratory, No. 17, Gracechurch Street. Referred to in Report as Ratin No. 1. (ii) " Ratin No. 2."—Obtained from same source. Referred to in Report as Ratin No. 2. (iii) "Ready Rat Relief."—Obtained from the Ready Rat Relief Virus Co., No. 23, Paternoster Square. Referred to in Report as Rat Virus No. 3. As the result of his examination of these three samples, Dr. Klein came to the following conclusions :— " From these experiments it follows that all these viruses are culturally and " morphologically closely related and belong to the group of bacteria which are " included in the group of "Paratyphoid," to which belong the Paratyphoid bacteria 27 " proper, the Danysz bacillus, Gaertner bacillus, the two microbes reported on " recently, viz., Seeker microbe and Liverpool virus. The close relation of the rat " virus microbes Ratin 1, Ratin 2 and Rat Viras 3 is also indicated by the fact that " an emulsion in broth of a recent gelatine culture, or a twenty-four hours broth " culture of either microbes Ratin 1, Ratin 2 and Rat Virus 3 are readily agglutinated " —almost instantly—by blood serum of a rabbit immunised with Secker microbe " or with blood serum of a rabbit immunised with Liverpool virus. It is clear from " this that at least a "group relation" exists between all these. It should be pointed out that the makers and sellers of vermin-destroying virus themselves seem to be somewhat uncertain with regard to the action of these preparations upon the human subject. In the case of the "Ready Rat Relief Virus" the directions for use state: " There need be no hesitation in using the fingers when preparing the bait, but " they should be free from wounds or sores." (The words in italics being printed in heavy black type.) The tins of Ratin No. 1, Ratin No. 2, and a bottle of " Liquid Ratin," bear the following instructions:—" The bacteria is harmless to human beings and " to all domestic animals, but it should be placed out of the reach of children " and sucking calves. Wash your hands after use." Again, the directions for use issued with the Danysz Virus also contain the words: "There need be no hesitation in using the fingers when preparing " the bait, but they should be free from wounds or sores. The virus has a " great advantage in being absolutely harmless to other animals." A bottle containing "Liquid Ratin," purchased at the Ratin Laboratory of No. 17, Gracechurch Street, was also sent to Dr. Klein, who reported as under:— “ From all these facts the conclusion is justified that ' Liquid Ratin ' contains “ microbes belonging to the paratyphoid group, fatal for mice, and as might be “ expected, distinctly pathogenic also for another rodent (guinea-pig). “ I should like to draw your attention to a paragraph printed on the label of “ the bottle of the ' Liquid Ratin,' which runs thus :— ' By experiments (sic.) it has been shown that the bacteria is harmless to ' human beings and to all domestic animals, but it should be placed out of the ' reach of children and sucking calves. Wash your hands after use.' “ It is not quite clear what is meant ' By experiments it has been shown' that “ the stuff is harmless to human beings, but it is, on the other hand, clear that the “ sellers of the material are not sure that it is not harmful to children, or that “ by contaminating human hands may not cause mischief." 28 In presenting these facts to the Sanitary Committee your Medical Officer pointed out that it is claimed that the use of each of these viruses brings about the same result, viz., the destruction of rodents and also that they are innocuous to domestic animals and human beings. As these claims, however, cannot be substantiated, it is extremely undesirable and even dangerous that they should be allowed to be sold and purchased by any person. If they are to be used they should be placed about with the greatest precaution and under skilled supervision. The Sanitary Committee directed a copy of your Medical Officer's Report to be sent, together with the Report of Dr. Klein's bacteriological examination, to the Local Government Board urging the desirability—in the public interest—of putting restrictions upon the sale of such poisons to members of the general public. In view of the above it may be interesting to quote a statement made in the House of Commons on the 4th November last in reply to the following question:— Mr. Ridsdale asked the President of the Local Government Board “ If his “ attention had been called to the report of Dr. Collingridge, Medical Officer “ of Health for the City of London, to the effect that a vermin poison, “ advertised as harmless to human beings but as disseminating bacteriological “ diseases to rodents, has caused serious illness to many persons ; and whether, “ in view of the danger to the public of the unrestricted sale of such poisons, “ he will consider the desirability of placing restrictions upon their sale." The President of the Local Government Boardreplied that the administration of the Acts relating to the sale of poisons rested with the Privy Council, and he would communicate with the Lord President. Full details of Dr. Klein's analyses of these viruses are given in the Medical Officer of Health's Reports, Nos. 116, 117 and 118. 29 CONTAMINATED MILK. The investigation into the character of the milk arriving in London for delivery to dealers in the City, which was commenced in 1902, and repeated in the years 1904, 1905 and 1906, was continued during 1907. Notwithstanding the strong feeling that has been aroused amongst all classes with regard to the importance of milk supplies being delivered under such conditions as to ensure freedom from contamination, the result of the several investigations of the milk supply of the City shows, that unless it is made obligatory upon farmers and others sending milk to town to take reasonable precautionary measures for ensuring purity, little or no improvement in this direction is likely to take place. The following are the results of the analysis of the series referred to:— 1904. 1905. 1906. 1907 Per cent. Per cent. Per cent. Per cent. Fairly clean and pure 48.7 68.2 80 54.2 Unclean 43.6 22.7 12 37.5 Tuberculous 7.7 9.1 8 8.3 With regard to tuberculous milk found in the last series, it will be seen there has been little variation in the percentage found. As in former years, in each case where the milk was found to be tuberculous, action was taken to ascertain if there were any cows suffering from that disease in the herd from which the milk was derived, and in each instance the bacteriological result was confirmed. These results not only demonstrate the value of bacteriology as a medium for determining the presence or otherwise of disease in milk, but also show that practically no precautions are taken by either farmers or wholesale dealers in London to ensure that the milk they are responsible for is not a vehicle for the conveyance of tuberculosis. They further show that while the best method of dealing with the control of milk is at its actual source, viz., the farm, there is no real difficulty in getting this effectually carried out on the spot by the Local Authority, as in each of the milks reported against above, upon bringing the facts to the notice of the local medical officer of health, steps were at once taken to improve the sanitary conditions prevailing on the farm in question. This indicates that there is no necessity for any central authority wasting time and money in inspecting farms in the country where their intrusion would be resented, and when the necessary supervision of any required work would be difficult if not impossible. 30 All that is necessary is for each Urban Authority to determine the condition of milk supplied to their district, and if found unsatisfactory, to present a report forthwith to the Local Authority in which the farm is situated, and also to the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries. However thorough the supervision of the farm may be, there are other factors conducing to prejudicially affect the wholesomeness of milk during its journey to, and arrival at, London. These adverse conditions are brought about by:— 1.—The unsuitable type of churn generally used. 2.—The fact that in many instances the churns are returned to the farmers from London unscalded and without any effort being made by the Dairy Company to see that they are cleaned at all. 3.—The use of unlocked and unsealed churns which admit dust and other impurities; and 4.—The system of mixing at the railway stations that is adopted by the Dairy Companies. Respecting (1) the shape of the churn usually used is ill-adapted for the purpose, as it affords an easy resting place for all kinds of dirt, which can easily find its way through the ventilation holes in the lid and so into the contents of the churn, moreover it lends itself to pilfering and the addition of water. The large wholesale dealers are being urged to adopt a more rational form of railway can (of which there are several on the market), the use of which would greatly minimise the danger of contamination of milk in its journey from the cow to the consumer. With regard to (2), the Dairy Companies have expressed their willingness to co-operate in the direction of improving the existing state of things. As to (3), it is remarkable that so many farmers are under the impression that railway companies object to milk being consigned in locked churns. As a matter of fact it is clearly stated in a leaflet, No. 110, published by the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries, that railway companies do not object to the locking system, and make no difference in the charge for such conveyance. An erroneous impression also prevails amongst the uninformed that milk readily sours in a closely-covered receptacle, whereas it has been ascertained that milk—if properly cooled after being drawn—will keep better in a closely-covered receptacle. With regard to (4) unfortunately, after all due precautions are taken, the effects of such are often absolutely nullified by the filthy conditions under which the objectionable system of mixing is carried on at railway stations. 31 It would be difficult to find a more unsuitable place for storing or mixing milk, as now largely obtains, than a railway platform. Uncovered churns of milk are frequently seen standing at these places for hours at a time, in some cases for six hours. Under such conditions the contents are exposed to all the various contaminations present in the dust of a large city. Here the milk is treated with a “rouser," which has often been allowed to stand on the dirty platform, and the mixing is conducted by the operator, can in hand, plunging his naked arm in the churn and abstracting the requisite amount and transferring it to another vessel, the measure used being also placed, at intervals, on the ground. The whole process of mixing is conducted under most unsuitable surroundings, and should be prohibited. Further, it absolutely destroys the value of a warranty which can nevertheless be successfully used as a defence to any prosecution for adulteration. As the railway companies do a very large business as milk carriers, they might very fairly be expected to provide suitable accommodation at the stations, where milk can be handled under proper hygienic conditions. The London County Council (General Powers) Bill of 1908, conferred upon Local Authorities in the metropolis certain powers with regard to removing from the register or to refuse to register the names of dairymen or purveyors of milk whose premises are unsuitable for the sale of milk thereon. This is a most useful provision, it being evident that even if the strictest precautionary measures are taken in furtherance of securing milk from contamination, both on the farm and in transit to town, they will be useless unless steps are taken for ensuring that it is stored and vended under sanitary supervision. The London County Council are again (1909) seeking legislative powers to take samples of milk at railway stations in the metropolis, with a view to prevent the distribution of dirty milk. This is a doubtful method of dealing with the question. It is obvious that it would be far better to eliminate any risk of danger at the source of supply, than to adopt expedients for control after the milk has left the farm. Piecemeal legislation of this kind is not, and never will be, effective, and the only satisfactory way of dealing with the question is for the Government to proceed at once with the promised general measure, which should repeal all former legislation in respect to milk regulation, and consolidate existing statutes into one comprehensive measure dealing with the whole subject. The control of milk supplies is especially a matter that should be under the supreme authority of the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries, whose business it should be to see that the Dairies, Cowsheds and Milkshops Orders are effectively carried out by those responsible for their administration. 32 TUBERCULOUS MILK. In November last some details were published with regard to milk samples examined for tuberculosis at the Lister Institute for the London County Council, under the provisions of Part IV. of the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1907, that became operative on 1st July, 1908. Two hundred and four samples were taken between 1st July, 1908, and 10th October, 1908, from milk carried by the following Railway Companies :— Samples taken. London and North-Western 60 Great Eastern 48 London and South-Western 60 Great Western 36 204 The examination of 92 of the above showed that 22, or 23.9 per cent., were tuberculous. Nineteen of the 22 cows supplying tuberculous milk were examined by a Veterinary Inspector, and 10 of them were reported to have tuberculous udders. These results are even more disturbing: than those found in the City. The danger of allowing such a state of things to exist is emphasised by the publication in January of this year of the Third Interim Report of the Royal Commission appointed to enquire into the relation between human and animal tuberculosis, which states that: “ The presence of tubercle bacilli in the milk of cows clinically “ recognisable as tuberculous confirms the opinion we expressed in our “ Second Interim Report, that the milk of such cows must be considered “ dangerous for human beings. “ The experiments which we have carried out with regard to the “ infectivity of the faeces of tuberculous cows were dictated by knowledge “ of the fact that dirt of various kinds from cows and the cow-shed is “ almost constantly present in milk as it reaches the consumer. Cows suffering from extensive tuberculosis of the lungs must discharge “ considerable numbers of bacilli from the air passages in the act of “ coughing, and some of the bacilli thus expelled may find their way “ into the milk. But all experiments indicate that the excrement of “ cows, obviously suffering from tuberculosis of the lungs or alimentary “ canal, must be regarded as much more dangerous than the matter 33 “ discharged from the mouth or nostrils. We have found that even in “ the case of cows with slight tuberculous lesions, tubercle bacilli in “ small numbers are discharged in the faeces ; whilst as regards cows “ clinically tuberculous, our experiments show that the faeces contain “ large numbers of living and virulent tubercle bacilli." A typical case of contamination of milk with faecal matter in the manner referred to above was brought to my notice on the 6th April, 1908, when an examination of the contents of a churn of milk deposited in the doorway of a shop revealed an extraordinary quantity of filthy extraneous matter, and the general condition of the milk being so obviously unfit for human consumption, the Inspector at once seized it and conveyed it to the Guildhall, where, after examination by me, it was condemned by the sitting Magistrate. Samples of the milk were submitted to both your Bacteriologist and Public Analyst. The former reported that the sample contained a considerable amount of dirt (faeces and clumps of leucocytes), and that the result of the action of the milk upon the guineapigs injected was as follows :— " St. Bartholomew's Hospital, E.C., " April 30th, 1908. "Re MILK SAMPLE No. 4. " P. M. " Guineapig, peritoneal :— " Abscess in omentum and on Liver, pus crowded with staphylococci, " no tubercle. Guineapig, subcutaneous :— " Local abscess, pus contains, in almost pure culture, staphylococci '• pyogenes. Viscera normal. (signed) E. Klein." Dr. Teed's chemical analysis showed that the sample was that of a poor milk, but it complied with the requirements of the Food and Drugs Act, although the sample was very dirty. It will be obvious that the results obtained by these experts fully justified the action of the Inspector in seizing the milk, and the Magistrate in condemning it. The wholesale dealer, as is customary in any question of dirty or unsound milk, was called upon to immediately furnish— i. A certificate from the Medical Officer of Health for the District in which the farm yielding the supply was situated as to the condition thereof, and ii. A certi6cate from a Veterinary Surgeon as to the health of the cows thereon. 34 After some delay these certificates were forthcoming, and proved conclusively that both the farm and the cows were in a perfectly satisfactory condition. It is possible that the filth found in the milk may have entered the churn during its journey from the farmer to the consumer, and this, I am sorry to say, is by no means an uncommon occurrence. If wholesale dealers would only realise the importance of safeguarding the purity of this article of diet, and the ease with which this can be accomplished by the adoption of sealed churns, then our hopes for a speedy reduction of the present rate of infantile mortality would be materialised. The summons was dismissed on technical grounds, the Magistrate holding that the milk was not in the possession of the man on whose doorway the churn was deposited. PUBLIC HEALTH (TUBERCULOSIS) REGULATIONS, 1908. By an Order dated the 18th December, the Local Government Board, in pursuance of Section 130 of the Public Health Act, 1875, as amended and extended by the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, and the Public Health Act, 1896, have provided for the notification to the medical officer of sanitary authorities of pulmonary tuberculosis (phthisis or consumption) occurring amongst the inmates of Poor Law Institutions, or amongst persons under the care of district (Poor Law) medical officers. Hitherto in the City the notification of these cases by district medical officers has been voluntary, but the sanitary authorities were not armed with any powers for preventing the spread of this disease, which is, as pointed out in the Order, not only endemic, but also of an infectious character. The issue of this Order is unquestionably a step in the right direction, as it cannot fail to bring home to the public, and the poorer classes particularly, the knowledge that consumption is infectious, and that it can therefore be fought and suppressed. The following is an abstract of the main provisions of the Order which came into operation on the 1st January of this year. By Article IV the medical officer of a Poor Law Institution must, within 24 hours of recognising symptoms of pulmonary tuberculosis in the case of a poor person who is an inmate of the Institution, notify the case to the medical officer of health of the sanitary district in which the person resided immediately before he enters the Institution. Article V directs similar notification is to be made in cases when a district medical officer recognises the disease in persons attended by him (i.e., outrelief patients), according to his agreement with the Guardians. 35 Under Article VI the superintending officer of a Poor Law Institution must notify the medical officer of health of the actual or intended place of destination and address at that place of any person leaving the Institution that has been the subject of notification. Article VII directs that a relieving officer must notify changes of patients' addresses to the medical officer of health of the district to which such person may move. By Article VIII notifications directed under the Order are to be paid for by the sanitary authority. The remuneration allowed to medical officers of a Poor Law Institution or a district medical officer will be at (he rate of one shilling, and in the case of a superintending officer of a Poor Law Institution or a relieving officer at the rate of threepence for every notification, but when in relation to one and the same case, two or more notifications have been sent by a Poor Law medical officer to one and the same medical officer of health, his remuneration will be sixpence for every notification after the first. Article IX provides that nothing in the regulations shall have effect so as to apply to, or to authorise anyone to put in force with respect to a person in relation to whom a notificat on has been made, any enactment which renders him, or any other person, liable to a penalty, or subject him to any restriction, prohibition, or disability affecting him or his employment, occupation, means of livelihood, or residence on the ground of his suffering from pulmonary tuberculosis. Subject to this qualification a sanitary authority, on the advice of their medical officer of health, may, in the case of a poor person notified as suffering from this disease:— i. “ Take all such measures or do all such things as are authorised, in “ any case of infectious disease, or of dangerous infectious disease, “ by any enactment relating to public health, and as have reference “ to the destruction and disinfection of infected articles, or to the “ cleansing or disinfecting of premises ; ii. “ Take all such measures or do all such things as are appropriate “ and necessary for the safe disposal or destruction of infectious “ material, produced and discharged, as a result of pulmonary “ tuberculosis ; and otherwise for the prevention of the spread “ of infection from any such material ; iii. “ Afford or supply all such assistance, facilities, or articles as, within “ such reasonable limits as the circumstances of the case require “ and allow, will obviate, or remove, or diminish the risk of 36 “ infection arising from the conditions affecting the use or “ occupation of any room, when used or occupied by the poor “ person as a sleeping apartment ; and iv. “ Furnish, for the use of the poor person, on loan, or otherwise, any “ appliance, apparatus, or utensil, which will be of assistance for “ the purpose of any precaution against the spread of infection." The sanitary authority, on the advice of their medical officer of health, is authorised to provide and publish or distribute in the form of placards, handbills, or leaflets, suitable summaries of information and instruction respecting pulmonary tuberculosis, and the precautions to be taken against spread of infection from that disease. The Board propose, in due course, to issue for the use of sanitary authorities and medical officers of health, a Memorandum by their medical officer, setting out the appropriate action that can be taken under the powers conferred under Article IX. referred to above. In these circumstances I advised that no action be taken by the Corporation in this direction until the receipt of the Board's Memorandum on the subject, and that in the meantime the precautionary measures for the prevention of phthisis now in operation in the City be observed as heretofore. THE SPITTING EVIL. In the Annual Report for 1907, in commenting on the nuisance created by spitting in railway carriages, attention was directed to the powers with which the railway companies are armed, under their bye-laws, for the repression of the evil. The fact that the Charing Cross, Euston and Hampstead Railway Co., on 16th December last, took proceedings at Marylebone against a passenger who had been seen to spit three times upon the floor of a railway carriage, and was ordered to pay five shillings, will, it is hoped, not be without effect as a warning to those addicted to this dangerous and disgusting practice. “CARRIERS” OF ENTERIC FEVER. With a view of protecting the public from the danger arising from the discharge from hospital of patients who, although in other respects perfectly healthy, may be carriers of infection, by reason of their still passing the bacillus typhosus in their stools and urine, the following arrangements have been made by the Local Government Board in connection with cases of this kind discharged from a hospital of the Metropolitan Asylums Board. 37 PROCEDURE. Samples of stools or urine, or both, to be sent to the Lister Institute, as follows:— 1. In all cases a sample of stools, and a sample of urine, to be sent monthly daring the first six months after discharge, the first samples being sent one month after discharge from hospital. 2. If the results, on examination of the sample, all prove negative during those six months, cease sending monthly samples, but send a sample of both stools and urine at the end of the next period of six months. If these also prove negative, no more samples to be sent. 3. If, in any case, the results are positive, continue sending monthly samples of stools and urine until further notice. The necessary apparatus for taking and sending by post samples of faeces and urine have been supplied by the Lister Institute, with instructions as regards their use. 38 CREMATION. It will be interesting to note if the policy of reducing the charge made by the Corporation for cremation at Ilford, as advocated in my'Annual Report for 1907, will result in popularising in the eastern parts of the Metropolis this method of disposing of the dead. In spite of the argument so constantly advanced that cremation should become general on the ground that it is the most hygienic method of destroying a mass of corruptive material, and therefore the most rational, there is still a great deal of prejudice to be overcome from the working classes before it becomes general. The appeal to their pockets may have some effect in the desired direction. The total number of cremations in the United Kingdom is steadily increasing, and amounted to 795 during the past year. The establishments of the Cremation Society at Woking and Golders Green maintain their popularity, but a classification of the position in life of those cremated at these places, sent by the Secretary of the Society, shows that the support of the movement is chiefly on the part of the wealthier and more intellectual part of the community. No new Crematoria were established in the United Kingdom during the year, but the local authorities at Nottingham, Bournemouth and Bristol are said to be considering the question.* The Cardiff City Council is statedf to have recently discussed the question of establishing a crematorium, when it was decided to take no action in the matter, the opponents of the scheme stating that there is a deep-seated religious objection to cremation. It would be a good thing if burial clubs and benefit societies were asked to advocate cremation amongst their members, and I cannot help thinking that both the Church and the medical profession might take a more active interest than they do in forwarding the movement. The majority of crematoria have been established by municipal authorities and quasi philanthropic societies, who advocate cremation, and therefore the charges should be better adjusted so as to suit the pockets of everyone. As a rule the charges are too high to attract the bulk of the people, and it is for this reason that the Corporation have reduced the inclusive charge for cremation from 41. 4s. to 3/. 3s. This fee includes all attendance after the body is placed on the catafalque table by the undertakers, the services of a clergyman if required, a plain urn for the remains, storage of the latter for three months. Failing the removal of the remains by the relatives within three months, they will be decently interred in land adjoining the Crematorium reserved for this purpose. * " Transactions of the Cremation Society of England for 1908." f " Lancet" 13/2/09. 39 The Cremation Society of England, the pioneers of cremation in this country, have also found it advisable to modify their rules. There is no disguising the fact that the religious objection is very slow in dying out, in spite of the decline of materialistic interpretation of Christian teaching. Again, the general public are not aware that religious rites are applicable to the burning as well as to the burial of bodies, and it is well to make more widely known sec. 11 of the Cremation Act, 1902, which provides that in the case of refusal by "the incumbent of any ecclesiastical parish to perform a " funeral service before, at, or after cremation, any Clerk in Holy Orders of " the Established Church, not being prohibited under ecclesiastical censure, " may, with the permission of the Bishop, and at the request of the executor " of the deceased person, or of the burial authority or other person having " charge of the cremation or interment of the cremated remains, perform such " service within such ground." It will be seen, therefore, that cremation does not in any way interfere with the religious routine of Christian burial. In reply to a circular letter sent to the Superintendents of the several Crematoria in the United Kingdom inviting their views with regard to the progress cf cremation, the following observations may be quoted:— The Honorary Secretary of the Scottish Burial Reform and Cremation Society, Glasgow, referring to the diminishing number of cremations at some places, states that :— " In Scotland we have been at a loss to understand this, and some of " our Directors are inclined to attribute it to the diminished death-rate ; " others attribute it to the fact that we have not for some time been " holding public meetings or doing anything to advertise the movement, " having rested content with providing the apparatus for those who " desire to avail themselves of it." He also notes that the prejudice against cremation is steadily diminishing, but that many people who are advocates of it do not take means to ensure their wishes being carried out after their death—in many cases the reason being that they do not wish to wound the feelings of relatives who hold different views. The Honorary Secretary of the Darlington Cremation Society also suggests that a declining death rate may have influenced a falling-ofF in the number of cremations. The Secretary of the Cremation Society of England, referring to a classified list of the occupations of 483 persons cremated at Golders Green and Woking Crematoria during 1908, remarks :— " It will be seen that the poorer classes are entirely unrepresented in " the foregoing figures, and until the advantages of cremation are " recognised by this section of the population (in which the greatest 40 " percentage of deaths occur in proportion) the progress of cremation " must of necessity be slow. " It would undoubtedly be difficult to comply with the Home Office " regulations in their present form to any extent among the lower classes, " as the particular medical information necessary could seldom be given " or the statutory requirements fulfilled. The question of expense may " in a good many cases militate against the adoption of cremation by the " industrial class, but it does not entirely account for their absence from " the above figures, as it is a notorious fact that the poor spend com" paratively more upon their funerals than the wealthier classes, and the " cost of cremation already compares favourably with burial carried out " with any degree of decency. Also it is alleged that the funeral trade " are opposed to cremation. But it must be borne in mind that under" takers as a body are just ordinary business men, and will supply what " the public demand. If the public insist on cremation there is no " reason to suppose that undertakers would not carry it out as readily as " any other method of disposing of the dead. " The true explanation of the slow progress of cremation seems to lie, " as suggested in the following extract from the ' British Medical " Journal,' in the fact that— " ' Cremation in this country is almost wholly confined to persons of " ' some intellectual distinction. The average citizen is still held in the " ' bondage of custom, in which sentiment has taken root so deeply as to " ' make it hard to eradicate.' " It is constantly brought to our notice that the expressed wishes of " persons for cremation are ignored upon their decease, in some cases by " the deliberate intention of the survivors, but a far greater proportion " from the fact that no adequate means are taken during lifetime to place " the desire for cremation upon record. It must be obvious that in cases " of doubt preference is given to that which is, at present, most " customary, i.e., Burial. In order to insure cremation at death the " party desiring it should communicate his wishes to his executors and " friends. It is not sufficient merely to insert a direction in a will, " because the will is rarely looked at until after the funeral. " Unfortunately, the law gives a free hand to unwilling executors, and " the only way of making instructions binding is to make any gift or " bequest subject to the wish being complied with." The Manager of the Liverpool Crematorium states : — " My experience is that the Declaration Form A. acts very inimically " to the progress of cremation, that the persons take alarm at anything " regarding a visit to a Justice of the Peace, or a solicitor, also that the " necessity of appearing before a Magistrate conveys the idea that there " must be something wrong in a process that requires such an appearance, " and that they are safer in resorting to burial—in fact, that, has barred " the way in several cases." 41 The Registrar and Superintendent of the Bradford Corporation Crematorium remarks:— " With regard to the question of cremation not becoming so popular as " was anticipated a few years ago, I find that opposition to it is " becoming rare, but it is giving place to a cause which may be even " worse than active opposition, i.e., indifference. This appears to me to " be the main reason why cremation is not taken up. 1 hear many " people say ' Yes, cremation is no doubt a good thing and the best way " ' of disposing of the dead from a sanitary point of view, but I don't •' ' care, they can do as they like when I am gone.' " It is far worse to argue or try to make people understand that they " are neglecting their duty when in this frame of mind, than if they were " opposed to cremation. " From the experience I have had lately, I feel this is the cause why " cremation is not becoming as popular as one would wish, and it will " take more breaking down than the opposition of a few years ago." The Clerk of the Headingly-cum-Burley (Leeds) Burial Board considers that public sentiment is still against this method of disposal of the dead. The Honorary Secretary of the Birmingham Crematorium writes :— " With reference to the progress of cremation, we find in Birmingham " that there are various reasons why cremation does not make such " rapid strides as is expected. In the first instance there are still large " numbers of people who are not aware of the advantages of cremation, " and I am therefore proposing shortly to prepare and deliver here a " lecture on the subject, illustrated with lantern slides. In the second " place I find that undertakers are strongly against cremation on account " of the reduction of their profit if they undertake one. . . . " Another factor which is against the advance of cremation is the " tremendous amount of red 'tape' with regard to the filling of the “ necessary forms. We find that relatives are very much against having " to give so many details at a time when they are distressed. There is " again, of course, the great sentimental objection which, until it is " removed, will always be a great drawback to cremation." With regard to the formalities required under the Cremation Act, referred to by the Manager of the Liverpool Crematorium, and the reference made to " red tape " by the Honorary Secretary of the Birmingham Crematorium, I can only repeat the views I have already expressed on this point, viz.* :— " It has been said that the retention of these regulations tends in a " great measure to retard the spread of cremation, but I am quite unable " to endorse this opinion. " Opponents of cremation would be only too ready to make adverse " use of any attempts to relax the present regulations which, very " properly, have been made exceedingly stringent, and it would certainly " be most unwise to lend any countenance to the suggested alteration." * Annual Report, Medical Officer of Health for the City of London, 1907. 42 CREMATIONS IN THE UNITED KINGDOM, 24 Years—1885-1908. These buildings, erected by the late Commissioners of Sewers and opened in January, 1885, stand upon ground formerly the site of several courts and alleys in the Ward of Portsoken. There are five "blocks" of Dwellings, severally named King's—Queen's— Prince's—North—and South, designed for occupation by persons of the artizan class; some of the ground floors being let as shops. The buildings contain 501 rooms, arranged in 241 tenements, and are occupied by 919* persons, compared with 941 in 1906. Sixty-two per cent, are adults, and thirty-eight per cent, children (under 14 years). This reduction in the number of inhabitants is owing to the steps taken to prevent overcrowding, which had been brought to my notice in the course of the year. It should be stated that where overcrowding was found to exist, it wasnot due to the abuse of sub-letting but mainly to the natural increase of families. The death rate for 1908 was 10.6 per 1,000 per annum [of which 40 per cent, was of children under 5 years of age]. The number of deaths per 1,000 births of children under one year was 103, as against 65 for the City generally. COMMON LODGING HOUSE. 116, Middlesex Street. The "Salvation Army Working Men's Hotel and Shelter," No. 116, Middlesex Street, is the only Common Lodging House in the City. It was opened on the 7th February, 1906, and provides accommodation for 453 persons. The charge for the Hotel portion is 5d. per night, and the Shelter 2d. per night, which includes a "bath and a bake" (disinfection of clothing). Food can be purchased cheaply. The premises have been periodically inspected during the year, and always found to be well managed and kept in a cleanly condition. 52 DRAINAGE PLANS. In the course of the year 371 plans for the proposed reconstruction of water-closets or drains were submitted for approval. With modification in certain cases, these were agreed to and the works carried out. In all cases where, on revision, it was found that the plans were not in accordance with the Corporation Bye-laws, the matter was submitted to the judgment of the Sanitary Committee. STREET GULLIES. Fewer complaints than usual were received during the summer months of nuisance of smells from street gullies in the public thoroughfares. In dry weather this is sometimes due to want of flushing, but in some cases the nuisance arises through faulty construction. The City Engineer is still giving special attention to this matter, and a large number of these appliances have been reconstructed and trapped, greatly to the relief of passers by and persons living in the vicinity. OVERCROWDING. Overcrowding is a nuisance dealt with under Section 2, (1), (e) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1901, and 17 cases were reported during the year. Notices of abatement being served, the overcrowding was discontinued in each instance. OVERCROWDING, &c., AT THE CENTRAL TELEGRAPH OFFICE. In September last, in consequence of complaints that the tube switch room on the ground floor of the above premises was overcrowded and imperfectly ventilated, and that such conditions were prejudicial to the health of the employes, the Inspector of the District was instructed to visit and report thereon. Some objection was taken at first to the Inspector's right of entry, which was not agreed to until he was provided with a written request from the Medical Officer of Health. As a result of the Inspector's report your Medical Officer, by arrangement, visited the premises, accompanied by Dr. Wilson, the Chief Medical Officer, of the General Post Office, on the 5th October, at 2 o'clock in the afternoon, 53 when the full staff were on duty. It was found on this occasion that there were some grounds for complaint, as the ventilation was defective, and there was also overcrowding. Complaints had also been made as to nuisance caused by the smell of cooking on the premises, and exaggerated charges made in the Press as to the conditions under which the staff were working; but although the conditions found certainly required amendment, the facts had been greatly overstated. It should be noted that the Postmaster-General claims that buildings of his Department are exempt from inspection by officers of Sanitary Authorities There can be no doubt that Government establishments should be subject to the same supervision by the Sanitary Authorities as other workplaces, and certainly Government employes should work under as healthy conditions as those of private individuals. Similar objection had been raised on previous occasions, but as the Postmaster-General stated that he is always disposed to meet the wishes of Local Authorities so far as they are reasonably practicable, and as it was understood that the Post Office Authorities were making arrangements for the transfer of this staff to better quarters, the question of right of entry in this instance was not of much importance. LEGAL PROCEEDINGS. In 13 cases only, out of 1,191 Notices issued during the year by the District Inspectors, was it found necessary in consequence of non-compliance to refer the matter to the City Solicitor with a view to taking such steps as he deemed necessary to ensure the required sanitary improvement being carried out. With 5 exceptions—now being dealt with—the Notices were subsequently complied with without the matter being taken into Court. Hempleman and Co. for the removal of Fish Offal expired on the 21st May, 1908, and on the recommendation of the City Engineer and your Medical Officer of Health was renewed for a further period of twelve months. The conditions of the contract require that the Contractor collect and remove, free of charge, between the hours of 7 p.m. and 9 a.m., all fish offal from the Corporation Markets and Fishmongers' premises in the City, to the entire satisfaction of the Medical Officer of Health, in specially constructed galvanised iron bins, of a pattern approved by that officer, and to thoroughly cleanse and disinfect such bins before returning them to the markets and shops. DRAINAGE, ROYAL EXCHANGE. The whole of the drainage of this building has now been reconstructed, and new connections made to the main sewers in Threadneedle Street, Royal Exchange Buildings and Cornhill. The old brick drain which formerly surrounded the Royal Exchange has been cut off and filled in with concrete, pedestal water-closets fixed, and a new system of ventilation provided. Sanitary accommodation has been provided for women, and a key given to each of the firms where such are employed. RAILWAY STATIONS. Periodical inspections were made of the sanitary accommodation provided at the twenty-five Railway Stations in the City. In 12 of them, the Companies they belong to have been called upon to effect sanitary improvements thereat. The defects, for the most part, have been due to lack of proper cleaning and to defective drains and sanitary fittings. In each case the necessary works have been satisfactorily carried out. INSPECTION OF SCHOOLS. The undermentioned schools in the district have been inspected, various defects found, and the following works executed:— Greystoke Place Day Training College.—New branch drains, water-closets and lavatory basins have been constructed. Moorfields Day Training College.—New iron drains laid, trapped and ventilated, new water-closets and waste pipes fixed. 55 REMOVAL OF HUMAN REMAINS. St. Mary-le-Bow, Cheapside. An application was made by the Rector and Churchwardens for the removal of the bodies from the crypt of this church. The church and crypt were visited frequently, but at none of these visits was any actual nuisance detected, though on general principles the removal is advisable. The number of bodies is estimated to be about 700. IRON DRAINS. With reference to the paragraph in my report for the year 1906 respecting the use of iron pipes in the reconstruction of drains in the City, the owners of property are now more fully recognising the great advantages of iron over stoneware, inasmuch as it is at this date the rule rather than the exception to relay drains in iron. The experience of my department goes to prove that the slightly increased cost is more than justified by the additional soundness and efficiency. HOUSES INFESTED WITH VERMIN. Four cases occurred where rooms in tenement houses were found to be infested with vermin. Upon the owners' attention being directed to the powers conferred upon the Corporation by 4 Ed. 7, cap. 244, sec. 20, they at once carried out the necessary disinfection and cleansing. The section referred to enacts that upon receipt of a certificate from the Medical Officer of Health, a Sanitary Authority is empowered to give notice in writing to the owner or occupier of verminous premises requiring him within a certain time, to be specified on the Notice, to cleanse the house, and if so required to remove the paper from the walls, and take such other steps for the destruction and removal of vermin as the case may require. Failing compliance with such Notice, the Sanitary Authority may do the work required themselves, and recover the expense as a civil debt from the person making the default. NUISANCE FROM WASHINGS FROM GAS PRODUCER PLANT. Of recent years the use of producer gas in the City for motive purposes has considerably extended, but this form of power is not without some disadvantage, as shown by several complaints of nuisance from gas washings inseparable from the process unless properly treated. 56 There can be no objection to this system of gas making for utilisation in engines instead of coal gas, provided no nuisance is created thereby, but the trouble in several cases has been caused by the abominable odour of the effluent which contains sulphuretted hydrogen. Further it is a direct contravention of the City of London Sewers Act, 1848, to discharge any gas washings into the public sewers. Section 76 is as under:— "And be it enacted, That if any Gas Company, or person making, "manufacturing, or supplying gas, shall at any time empty, drain, or "convey, or permit or suffer to be emptied, drained, or conveyed, or to run "or flow, any washings, or foul or waste water, or any noisome or "offensive liquid, substance, or thing which may arise or be produced in "the making of gas, into any public sewer or drain, every Gas Company "or person so offending, shall forfeit and pay the sum of Two hundred "Pounds for each and every day during which any such washing, or "foul or waste water or other noisome or offensive liquid, substance, or "thing, shall be permitted or suffered to flow into any public sewer "or drain." It has been found by experiment that the odour of sulphuretted hydrogen with which these gas washings are charged, can be practically eliminated by the use of' permanganate of potash, the addition of which to the washings in a settling tank producing an effluent to which little or no exception can be taken. Such treatment abated the nuisance in each of the cases reported last year, and on the advice of the City Engineer the discharge of the treated effluent was limited to the hours between 8 p.m. and 8 a.m. Each case, however, of this kind will be dealt with on its merits, and the effluent examined from time to time. No further complaints have been received. REMOVAL OF CHEMICAL AND DRUG FACTORY. During the past year one of the largest of these firms have removed their factory to an outlying district. While they had taken precautions and carried out a number of works to prevent the emission of effluvia, the position of the factory, surrounded as time went on by new and higher buildings, the nature of the business rendered them very objectionable to, and caused much unpleasantness with, their neighbours. 57 OLD WALBROOK SEWER. The remaining portion of this sewer running under the premises known as Joiners' Hall Buildings, between Upper Thames Street and Greenwich Street, was found to be in a very bad condition upon some alterations to a drain which ran into it being taken in hand; it was practically an unventilated cesspool, with a considerable quantity of sewage lying in the bottom. The drain was connected to the sewer under the public way in Joiners' Hall Buildings, and the old sewer filled in and demolished. This completes the abolition of this sewer which formerly, for nearly the whole of its length, ran under private property. METROPOLITAN SEWERS AND DRAINS BILL, 1908. Combined Drainage. This Bill, referred to in my Report for 1907, was initiated by the St. Pancras Borough Council, and met with the general support of the Metropolitan Borough Councils and the City of London. It was introduced with a view of amending the law relating to combined drainage in the Metropolis which had been for many years in a most unsatisfactory state, and cast an unduly heavy burden on the ratepayers. The Bill, which was introduced by a private Member, was read a first time on 5th March, 1908, and on 30th January, 1908, the Court of Common Council adopted a recommendation of the Sanitary Committee that the draft Bill be approved and the City Members requested to support the same in Parliament. In a letter dated 13th August, 1908, it was pointed out by the promoters of the Bill that the experience of the previous Session showed that there was little prospect of a private Member's Bill dealing with the difficulties created by the definition of "sewer" and "drain" becoming law without the co-operation of the Government, and suggesting that representation should be made to the President of the Local Government Board urging him to take over the Bill on behalf of the Government. Both the Sanitary and Streets Committees, to whom this question had been referred, concurred therein, and Mr. Remembrancer was instructed to consider the advisability of adopting the suggestion. It ended, however, in the Bill being dropped before obtaining a second reading. 58 SANITARY DEFECTS AND INFECTIOUS DISEASE. All premises where infectious disease was notified were inspected with reference to drainage, soil and waste pipes, water-closets, water supply, ventilation, &c., and of the 71 cases investigated in 1908, it has been necessary to entirely reconstruct the sanitary system in 8 instances, and to make amendments to soil, waste pipes, and W.C's in 34 others. HOUSE REFUSE REMOVAL. The City Engineer informs me that the refuse removed from the City in 1908 consisted of 28,712 cartloads of street sweepings and slop, and 52,758 loads of house and trade refuse. Total, 81,470 loads. The use of the Destructor was discontinued in September, 1905, since which date all refuse has been removed by barge from Letts' Wharf to the Corporation land on Hornchurch Marshes, where it is dealt with. SUMMER FLUSHING OF COURTS. All courts and narrow passages throughout the City were regularly flushed during the summer months under the direction of the City Engineer, who informs me that in this service and the washing of the main streets last year no less than 75,581,011 gallons of water were used. Disinfectants were also freely applied where necessary. The condition of the approaches to Billingsgate Market and the Custom House, as heretofore, required special attention, there having always been a difficulty in keeping the thoroughfares in the neighbourhood of these places free from the nuisance caused by the drippings from the fish carts, especially in hot weather.
