Smoke nuisances were reported at fifteen premises during 1908. The number of observations recorded amounted to 542, as against 341 noted at twelve premises in 1907. The service of Notices upon offenders resulted in the abatement of the nuisances in all cases, and therefore there has been no occasion to take further action in the matter. 62 In a letter dated the 10th March, 1908, the London County Council invited the views of the Corporation with regard to proposals* for conferring additional powers upon the London County Council and the several Metropolitan Boroughs for the prevention and abatement of smoke nuisances. The following were the suggestions made by the Council, viz. (a) to (f), your Medical Officer's comments thereon being added thereto in each case:— (a) That dense smoke discharged from any building, vessel, stationary or locomotive engine, place or premises (other than a private dwelling house) within the area of the City and the County of London and the surrounding urban districts to a distance of twelve miles of Charing Cross, or upon the waters of the Port of London and its tributaries within such area, shall be a nuisance, for which a fine not exceeding £10 may be imposed, with cumulative penalties for subsequent offences at the discretion of the magistrate. The substitution of the word "dense" for "black" will enable local authorities to abate many smoke nuisances that under existing circumstances it is difficult to deal with on account of its not being "black." (b) That the Council shall have power to make Bye-laws, subject to the approval of the Home Office or the Local Government Board. i. To bring under the terms of the provision set out in conclusion (a) other instances of avoidable smoke nuisance. ii. To prescribe and revise from time to time a standard of smoke nuisance to further define emissions of smoke to be liable to penalties. iii. To prohibit the adoption in new or reconstructed factories, premises or buildings, or any furnace, or grate or other fuel consuming apparatus of a type which shall have been proved to the satisfaction of the Council to produce an unnecessary amount of smoke. iv. To prescribe any additional measures or requirements necessary for the suppression and prevention of smoke nuisance. v. To provide a penalty for the breach of any such Bye-law not exceeding the penalty specified in conclusion (a). The Bye-laws would cover such an extensive range of subjects for regulation that they might greatly affect the interests of manufacturers with regard to iii. and of owners of private dwellings. Although, in the main, Bye-laws for such purposes may be deemed desirable, they will have to be very carefully considered by the proposed confirming authorities. *On the 12th March this year some modification of these proposals were considered at a conference of the Public Control Committee of the London County Council with representatives of the Corporation of London and the Metropolitan Borough Councils, held at the County Hall, Spring Gardens. 63 (c) That exemption from liability to penalties implied or expressed in favour of Crown property or premises of electric power corporations be repealed. If such exemptions are repealed it will furnish a useful precedent. (d) That it shall be the duty of the several Sanitary Authorities to enforce the provisions of the law and any bye-laws made by the Council. The Local Sanitary Authority should be the only body, except in cases of default, to enforce the suggested provisions and bye-laws in their own district. (e) That the Council shall have joint authority with the several administrative bodies to enforce the law, and any bye-laws within the area as above defined, with power to recover from the Sanitary Authority when it has been in default, the expenses of proceedings not recovered by order of a Court and not incurred in unsuccessful proceedings. It is inadvisable that the County Council should have joint authority with Sanitary Authorities, but they might be authorised to act in case of default in Metropolitan Boroughs. (f) That the County Council shall be the paramount authority for the whole area above defined, and as such shall have power to expend money for the purposes of i. Promoting a knowledge of the principles of (a) Combustion of fuel. (b) Furnace and grate construction. (c) Stoking. ii. Encouraging the study of the scientific heating of houses or the use of smokeless fuels, with a view to the reduction of nuisances from the open fire grate. iii. Determining generally the best means for securing the prevention of smoke in the consumption of fuel. With regard to (f) your Medical Officer pointed out that this is rather a question for the Committee to exercise their judgment as to whether it would be judicious to authorise the expenditure of the ratepayers' money on experimental work in the direction indicated. 64 Further, whatever additional legislative powers are sought by the Council, the autonomy of the City should be reserved in every respect, and the County Council should have no jurisdiction in any circumstances within the City. It was further noted that the suggestion contained in the Council's proposals constitute another amendment of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, and the time had arrived when it should be amended and consolidated by the inclusion therein of the many sanitary enactments introduced from time to time in the County Councils (General Powers) Acts, and that in future any public health legislation connected with the Metropolis as a whole should be promoted by the Government of the day. The Committee concurred in the views expressed above, and a copy of these observations were directed to be forwarded for the information of the Council. HYGIENE OF HAIRDRESSERS' AND BARBERS' SHOPS. Henry Theobald, 201, Bishopsgate Street Without. Joseph Witte, 151, Aldersgate Street. LONDON COUNTY COUNCIL (GENERAL POWERS) ACT, 1908. This Act, which in its progress through Parliament has been the subject of several reports by your Medical Officer, received the Royal Assent on the 1st August, 1908, and became immediately operative. The opposition of the Corporation to certain clauses was successful. At the second reading of the Bill in the Commons, upon the President of the Local Government Board giving an undertaking that on an early occasion he would bring in a general Bill dealing with the milk supply of the county, the Police and Sanitary Committees were instructed to strike out from what was then Part II. of the Bill all clauses dealing with the milk supply other than the "model clauses." This instruction resulted in the whole of Part II., which deals with dirty or unwholesome milk, being struck out, as the "model clauses" deal only with milk in relation to tuberculosis. Your Medical Officer reported at length on this Act in a report dated the 27th October, 1908. The Sanitary provision in Part II. of the Act confers the following additional powers upon the Corporation:— Section 5: To remove from or refuse to register names of dairymen whose premises are for any reason unsuitable for the sale of milk thereon. Section 6: Power to appoint and pay the salaries of suitable women to be known as health visitors, for the purpose of giving advice to persons as to the proper nurture, care and management of young children and the promotion of cleanliness. 66 Section 7: To require the owners of premises let as tenement houses, after the passing of the Act, to provide suitable accommodation for the cooking of food for the use of each family occupying such house on the storey or one of the storeys in which are situate the rooms or lodgings in the separate occupation of such family. Section 8: To enforce the following sanitary regulations for premises used for sale, &c., of food for human consumption. (a) No urinal, water-closet, earth-closet, privy, ashpit or other like sanitary convenience shall be within such room, shop or other part of a building, or shall communicate therewith except through the open air or through an intervening ventilated space. (b) No cistern for supplying water to such room, shop or other part of a building shall be in direct communication with and directly discharge into any such sanitary convenience. (c) No drain or pipe for carrying off fæcal or sewage matter shall have any inlet or opening within such room, shop or other part of a building. (d) No such room, shop or other part of a building shall be used as a sleeping place, and so far as may be reasonably necessary to prevent risk of the infection or contamination of any such article as aforesaid, no sleeping place shall adjoin such room, shop or other part of a building and communicate therewith, except through the open air or through an intervening ventilated space. (e) Refuse and filth, whether solid or liquid, shall not be deposited or allowed to accumulate in any such room, shop or other part of a building, except so far as may be reasonably necessary for the proper carrying on of trade or business. (f) Due cleanliness shall be observed in regard to such room, shop or other part of a building, and all articles, apparatus and utensils therein, and shall be observed by persons engaged in such room, shop or other part of a building. Section 9 empowers the Corporation to make Bye-laws with respect to the businesses of A vendor of Fried Fish, A Fish Curer, A Rag and Bone Dealer, or any of them, and with respect to the premises in or upon which any such business is carried on, and the apparatus, utensils and appliances used for the purpose of or in connection with any such business. 67 Section 12 contains an extremely useful provision, as it gives sanitary authorities or any officer or person duly authorised by them the right to enter at all reasonable times any house, building, or premises in the district of such Sanitary Authority for the purpose of examining whether there is any contravention of the foregoing provisions of the Act, or of any Bye-law made thereunder, or any non-compliance with the requirements of any notice given thereunder respectively. This power to enter by a Sanitary Authority for the purpose of ascertaining if there is any contravention of the law was first introduced in the London County Council (General Powers) Act of 1904. It was then an entirely new departure, and one of great importance to Sanitary Authorities. Similar powers should be conferred upon Sanitary Authorities in relation to all nuisances. At present, under the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, power of entry is only given in cases when there is reasonable cause to believe there is some contravention of that Act or of a Bye-law made thereunder. In pursuance of Section 5 of the Act, giving the Corporation power to remove from or to refuse to register the names of purveyors of milk whose premises are unsuitable for the sale of milk thereon, notice was given, upon the recommendation of your Medical Officer, to the proprietors of a number of such places in the City where milk was sold, that unless they carried out such arrangements as were necessary to enable them to comply with Sections 6 and 23 of the regulations made by the Corporation under the Contagious Diseases (Animals) Act, 1878, and the Dairies, Cowsheds and Milkshops Orders, 1885, their names would be removed from the Register, and the sale of milk on the premises prohibited. The places in question were small general shops crowded with miscellaneous goods under conditions that rendered them quite unfitted for the storage of milk. Up to the end of 1908 two of these were removed from the Register, five were retained upon the notice being complied with, and many others have since been dealt with. 68 NATIONAL CONFERENCE ON INFANTILE MORTALITY. This Conference was held at Caxton Hall, Westminster, on the 24th and 25th March. 1908. It was attended by your Medical Officer and Miss Pole (one of your Women Inspectors) as delegates to represent the Corporation in accordance with the Resolution of the Court of Common Council of the 27th February, 1908. It was explained that the Conference was the outcome of meetings in London, Liverpool, Leeds, Sheffield, and various other places. During the Conference a number of interesting papers dealing with the question of Infantile Mortality were read and discussed. The following Resolutions were passed:— I. That this Conference expresses its appreciation of Parliament having passed the Notification of Births Act, 1907, and urges upon all local authorities the importance of adopting the Act and appointing qualified women to carry out its provisions. II. That, in order to combat the prevalent ignorance in wastage of infant life and injury to health of many survivors, the Conference urges upon the Board of Education, and upon educational authorities generally, the importance of securing to all girls in every grade of school a satisfactory training in domestic and personal hygiene and the duties of womanhood. III. That the Conference welcomes most gladly the Children Bill introduced into the House of Commons by Mr. Herbert Samuel, M.P., for the amendment of the Infant Life Protection Act, 1897, and recommends the Executive Committee to ask that the Bill be specially amended to include the case in which one child only is put out to nurse for reward, and that the Bill be further amended to empower local authorities to charge upon the rates the cost of carrying out the existing statutory provisions for the prevention of cruelty to children. IV. That the Conference, being convinced of the injury done to infant life and health through infants being taken into public-houses, urges upon Parliament the necessity of providing such legislation as will prevent this. V. That this Conference urges upon the Boards of Guardians the advisability of making greater use of their existing powers to adopt, under the Poor Law Act, the children of incorrigible parents. VI. That all preparations offered or sold as foods or drugs for infants should be certified by a Government Analyst as non-injurious, and that each packet should contain its analysis. 69 VII. That in view of the unsatisfactory conditions under which milk is produced and distributed, the Conference being of opinion that immediate amendment of the law is desirable, urges upon Parliament the necessity of at once taking such steps as are necessary to effect this. VIII. That the Conference re-affirm the resolution of the former Conference, that the Midwives Act, 1902, be extended to Scotland and Ireland. IX. That the Conference continue as a Committee to give effect to the foregoing Resolutions, with power to remit to an Executive Committee to carry out the same. With regard to Resolutions Nos. I., III., IV., VI. and VII.— I. The Corporation has adopted the Notification of Births Act, see p. 91. III. The suggested extension of the provisions of the Infant Life Protection Clauses was adopted in the Children Act, see p. 89. IV. This suggested provision was embodied in the Licensing Bill of last year. VI. There can be no question that the unrestricted sale of soothing syrups and similar preparations is a fruitful source of danger to infant life. Proprietary food preparations for infants are in many cases quite unfitted for infant use. It should be made an offence to sell either medicines or food for infants that are not labelled with a notice declaring the nature and constituents of such articles. The view of the Right Hon. Lord Robert Cecil on this point may be quoted here with advantage, as it shows very clearly the need for an alteration of the law. "It is forbidden to sell articles which are adulterated so as to be unfit for the use "for which they are sold. That applies to ordinary articles of food, it applies to "fertilisers and feeding stuffs; but there is one department to which, oddly enough, "it does not apply, and that is the sale of food for infants. That is really a matter "of enormous importance. I am told, and I believe on perfectly good authority, "that there are a mass of these infants foods which are little better than concentrated poison for the unhappy children who consume them. I do hope that if we "are prepared, as we are in the House of Commons, to protect the cattle, and even "to protect the fields which grow the food for the cattle, we might, at any rate, go "as far as to say that infant food which is poisonous to infant life should not be "sold in the markets of this country." (22nd November, 1907.) VII. With regard to the unsatisfactory conditions under which milk is produced and distributed, the Government is pledged to legislate for an improved milk supply at an early date. 70 On the '26th February of this year the Prime Minister received a deputation of Medical Officers and others who presented the resolutions referred to above, passed at the Conference on Infantile Mortality. It may be interesting to quote the observations thereon made by the President of the Local Government Board on this occasion:— "The President remarked on the fact that since the Conference had commenced "its labours two years ago there had been, in 1908, 15,000 more children's lives "saved to the State. That was a very cheering fact. At the Local Government "Board he had set up a special department to inquire into the quality of foods, "and the £2,000,000 worth of imports was claiming their attention at the moment. "He had referred to the question of the quality and character of baby foods to two "doctors at the Local Government Board. The deputation asked that all baby foods "should be certified by a Government analyst, but the great difficulty in this "connection was that food might be given to a child from one to five years of age "which, if given to an infant between birth and five and six months of age, would "mean death. It was a difficult subject, but he trusted a way out would be found. "They were getting more help than they had hoped from the Notification of "Tuberculosis Act, which came into operation this year. As they found infant "mortality in the smallest houses where there was the largest number of children in "one room, they trusted that two or three years' operation of the new Housing Bill, "which must become law this year, would make some considerable alteration. "Then, if the Milk Bill passed, as he hoped it would, the children of this country "would certainly have something to thank the Government for."—("Daily Telegraph," 26th February, 1909.) 71 ROYAL SANITARY INSTITUTE CONGRESS. This Congress was held at Cardiff from the 15th to 18th July, and was attended by Frederick Link, Esq., J.P., and Sigismund Pollitzer, Esq., respectively Chairman and late Chairman of the Sanitary Committee, and by Dr. Williams, Acting Medical Officer for the City, as delegates of the Corporation of London. A Conference of Municipal Representatives was held, at which a paper was read on the Notification of Births. This was of great interest, giving the experience gained at a large City like Glasgow. There they had originated a system of voluntary visitors, and had established five consultation rooms, at three of which milk was provided for the feeding of infants. The discussion which followed was strongly in favour of the efficiency of the Notification of Births Act, and it was pointed out that even if it had no other effect than the prevention of ophthalmia in children, owing to the want of early treatment, the passing of the Act would be fully justified. There was a discussion on the construction and management of Crematoria. Many details were given of the Crematorium at Leicester, and an opportunity was taken during the discussion by your Acting Medical Officer of giving some particulars with regard to the working of the City Crematorium at Ilford. In the section on Sanitary Science and Preventive Medicine some interesting papers were read—notably one on the examination of sausages and their hygienic preparation. The result of twelve bacteriological examinations showed that the sausages contained the bacillus coli in many instances, varying from 100 to 120,000 per gramme of sausage. Stress was laid on the condition of the premises where the sausages were prepared, and one of H.M. Inspectors of Factories read from a Blue Book an account of visits which he had paid to eight places in the Southern District where sausages were made. His inspections revealed an extremely unsatisfactory state of affairs. Sausages were made in cellars, which apparently had never been whitewashed, the walls were covered with dirt, and the floors uneven and studded with pools of stagnant, dirty water. Papers were also read at the Conference of Medical Officers of Health with regard to the necessity of establishing a standard of cleanliness in all milking competitions at Agricultural shows. At first sight it would appear that, if anywhere, a standard of cleanliness would be observed at such competitions. The writer of the paper had visited a large Agricultural Show, and gave the results of his observations, which showed that there was a great want of even ordinary methods of cleanliness, and in one instance he noted faecal matter falling into the pail, which added considerably to the weight of the pail of milk, and if this be the state of affairs at an agricultural show under the immediate observation of the public, it is not difficult to imagine the insanitary and unhygienic method of obtaining milk from cows in small dairy farms in the country. 72 FACTORY AND WORKSHOP ACT, 1901. FACTORIES, WORKSHOPS AND WORKPLACES. The work of inspection has continuously increased since the Act came into force in 1902. Under the Act, all Local Authorities are required to keep a Register of Workshops, and to transmit a copy of the Annual Report of their Medical Officer of Health upon the subject to the Home Office, this being in addition to the copy of the Annual Report required to be sent to the Local Government Board as heretofore. In addition to the Register of Workshops, Registers are also kept of Factories and Workplaces. Every Local Authority also has definite duties with regard to "Outworkers," and the work under this heading is bound to largely increase when the projected legislation respecting "Sweated Trades" is carried into effect.* The steady increase in the number of this class of workers dealt with is shown at page 79, under the heading of "Outworkers." The reference book for the year contains 5,049 entries of matters affecting Workshops, &c., referred to the staff specially engaged upon this work. The names and addresses of 16,909 Outworkers were sent to various Local Authorities; whilst over 2,900 notices were issued for various reasons. The figures for the year show an increase of 99 Premises registered, representing 405 separate Workrooms, &c. Under a General Powers Act, passed in 1908, affecting London generally, the Corporation possesses additional powers with respect to certain trades, including those of a Vendor of Fried Fish, a Fish Curer, and a Rag and Bone Dealer, which, however, are not numerous within the City. As mentioned in previous Reports, the sanitary defects generally found in workshops may be classified under the following headings:— Want of cleansing. Insufficient ventilation. Overcrowding. The lack of separate and suitable water-closet accommodation for both sexes. Under the Factory Act, the occupier of a Workshop is bound to give notice to H.M Factory Department of the establishment of any Workshop, but no such notice is required to be given to the Local Authority, and the information subsequently reaches the Local Authority through the Factory Department. As Local Authorities have been made specially responsible for Workshops, it is desirable that notice of the existence of any new Workshop should also be given to them and so enable any sanitary defects to be remedied without unnecessary delay. With few exceptions, the whole of the premises registered were visited during the year, and there is but little to add to previous Reports. The principal difficulty now met with is the provision of separate and suitable water-closet accommodation for the sexes. When businesses change hands, or structural alterations are carried out—or both—this item is often lost sight of. The male members of the staff are said to be "paid to go out" to find accommodation at public conveniences, &c. Such arrangements are not satisfactory, and do not comply with the requirements of the Act. The occupiers of several restaurants, &c., submitted plans for approval, and it cannot be too widely known that this is the only proper course to adopt in the best interests of the parties concerned, who are thereby enabled to comply at once with the necessary requirements at a minimum of expense. Every effort has been made to give effect to the clauses of the Act respecting Outworkers, and in no instance was a prosecution necessary. The Lists due on February 1st and August 1st respectively, were all obtained by March 2nd and August 22nd. This is satisfactory, more especially so, as the City has at present to deal with by far the largest number of outworkers' addresses of any Metropolitan District. 80 There are but few "Home" workers in the City. The total number of Outworkers' premises registered is 214, only 43 of which can in any way be described as occupied by "Home" workers. The remainder are ordinary workshops, and appear on the Register of Workshops. The "Home" workers are visited by your Women Inspectors periodically. See page 85. Much difficulty was at first experienced in procuring lists from employers, and it was only after printed notice was issued to each firm concerned a few days before the Lists became due that an improvement took place. COL LING RIDGE, Medical Officer of Heal*. Note.—The Factory and Workshop Act, 1901 (s. 132), requires the Medical Officer of Health in his Annual Report to the District Council to report specifically on the administration of that Act in workshops and workplaces, and to send a copy of his Annual Reports to the District much of it as deals with this subject, to the Secretary of State (Home Office). If the Annual Report is presented otherwise than in print, it is unnecessary to include in the copy sent to the Home Office the portions which do not relate to factories, workshops, laundries workplaces or homework. The duties of Local Authorities and the Medical Officer of Health under the Act of 1901 are detailed in the Home Office Memorandumof December l904. A further Memorandum, on the Home Work Provisions of the Factory Act, was issued to all District 84 WOMEN SANITARY INSPECTORS. FACTORIES. The majority of women who work in factories in the City are employed in the Clothing Trades. Out of a total of 10,236 women workers, 5,500 are shown by the Factory Register to be employed in some branch of clothing work. Under this heading are included: Millinery, Blouse-making, Underclothing, Feather-dressing, &c. The number of workrooms in these trades on the Register is 399. In Printing, Bookbinding, and Envelope-making, 3,941 women are employed, and 662 women work in Tobacco factories. The other trades represented include Furniture and Upholstery, Tea-packing, Silk-winding, and two factory Laundries. In Factories the Sanitary Authorities deal chiefly with sanitary accommodation, other health questions being within the purview of H.M. Inspectors. 586 visits were paid by your Women Inspectors to factories in which women are employed. In many instances the sanitary accommodation was found to be dirty and defective, and a large number of cases of unsuitable or insufficient accommodation were reported, and where structural alterations were required were dealt with by the District Inspectors. WORKSHOPS. The workshops in which women are employed are nearly all places where clothing of some kind is made. The total number of these workshops (including Millinery, Blouses, Tailoring, Feathers, Artificial Flowers, Mantles, Underclothing, &c.), is 1,264; 764 being workshops in which men and women are employed, and in 500 women only are employed. The number of women employed in these trades is 8,041. Other trades represented are: Umbrellas, Leather goods, Stationery, Furniture and Upholstery, Florists, Perfumery, Druggists, &c., and two Laundries. The total number of women employed in all workshops (including domestic workshops) is 10,491. The number of inspections made during the year is as follows:— Initial Inspections 185 Periodical Inspections 1,550 Re-inspections 79 Total 1,814 85 The defects found were chiefly in relation to dirty walls and ceilings, dirty and defective sanitary arrangements, and insufficient ventilation. A few cases of absence of means of heating were discovered, and have been referred to H.M. Chief Lady Inspector of Factories. WORKPLACES—(Kitchens). Forty new Kitchens of Restaurants or Tea-rooms have been inspected and placed on the Register during the past year. The number of periodical inspections made was 285. As usual, many basement kitchens were found to be insufficiently ventilated. Other defects found were dirty walls and ceilings, defective kitchen floors, and unsuitable, dirty, and defective sanitary accommodation. 198 re-inspections were paid in connection with the work required. OUTWORKERS. The lists of outworkers sent in by employers during 1908 contain 624 entries of premises in the City where work is carried on. As one outworker may be employed by several employers, each of whom may send in his name twice in one year, it is not surprising to find that these 624 entries represent only 214 outworkers. Of this number 106 had been notified in previous years, and the remaining 108 were newly notified as outworkers, though the majority of them were already on the Workshop Register. In view of the fact that that part of the Home Office Return which deals with outworkers is headed "Home Work," it is necessary to point out that the actual number of outworkers carrying on work in their own homes is only 43, made up as follows:— Thirteen employ persons other than members of their own families, and appear on the Register of Workshops; twenty-five employ only members of their own families, and are classed as having " domestic workshops." Five are "occasional workers," i.e., those whose "labour is "exercised at irregular intervals and does not furnish the whole or "principal means of living to the family," and who are consequently exempt from the provisions of the Factory and Workshop Act. There has been no occasion to take action under section 108 or sections 109 and 110 in Factory and Workshop Act, 1901, with regard to any outworker's premises during the past year. The complaints which have been received during the past year with regard to insanitary conditions in offices in which women clerks are employed may be grouped under the following headings:— Absence of any sanitary accommodation. No separate sanitary accommodation. Unsuitable or defective sanitary accommodation. Offices overcrowded. Offices in dirty condition. These have all been investigated, and the defects found were, in every instance, remedied. One complaint which was received of absence of any means of heating in an office was, on enquiry, found to be unwarranted, the whole building being efficiently heated by hot-water pipes, and the rooms were well ventilated and in good sanitary condition generally. The inspection, however, was not without value, as in the course of it a condition of dampness (due to condensation) was discovered in the conveniences set apart for lady clerks. On the Manager's attention being called to this the necessary steps were taken to remedy the nuisance. In addition to the offices inspected an investigation has been made as to the sanitary accommodation provided for women serving in tobacconists' and other stalls at railway stations. It was found that in some instances these employes had free access to the Ladies' Waiting Rooms at the Stations. In the case of Stations where there is no Ladies' Waiting Room the firms concerned were communicated with, and after some correspondence a satisfactory arrangement was arrived at. Your Women Inspectors have also dealt with several complaints which have been made with regard to the sanitary accommodation provided for women employed as Shop Assistants. LADIES' WAITING ROOMS AT RAILWAY STATIONS. The usual inspections of Ladies' Waiting Rooms at Railway Stations have been carried out, involving one hundred and twenty-three visits. The defects found were all of a minor character, and were promptly remedied on being brought to the notice of the Railway Companies concerned. The marked improvement in the condition of these waiting rooms since they were first systematically inspected in 1902 has been well maintained. One Ladies' Waiting Room has been closed during this year, viz., the second class one at Holborn Viaduct Station. 89 UNDERGROUND CONVENIENCES FOR WOMEN. The usual inspections of underground conveniences have been carried out. There are at present 13 of these conveniences for women in the City, situated at:— Aldersgate Street (North). Aldersgate Street (General Post Office). Aldgate Bishopsgate Street Without. Circus Place (Finsbury Circus). Farringdon Street (North). Farringdon Street (South), Ludgate Circus. Golden Lane. Guildhall Buildings. Holborn. Minories. Upper Thames Street (near London Bridge). West Smithfield. Any defects found have been reported to the Engineer. CHILDREN ACT, 1908. (8. EDW. 7. Ch. 67.) This Act received the Royal Assent on the 21st December, 1908, and will come into force on 1st April, 1909. The Corporation is the Authority within the City for carrying out Part I. of the Act dealing with Infant Life Protection, which provides: Section 1. That when a person undertakes for reward the nursing and maintenance of one or more infants under the age of seven years, apart from their parents, or having no parents, he shall, within 48 hours from the reception of any such infant, give notice in writing thereof to the Sanitary Authority. In cases when the period for which the infant is received is for 48 hours or less, this Section does not apply. Under Section 2, it is made the duty of the local authority to put this part of the Act into execution, and to make inquiry with regard to persons within their district undertaking the nursing and maintenance of infants coming within the scope of the Act. The local authority is further charged with the duty of seeing that such infants are visited from time to time by duly appointed inspectors of either sex. Provision is also made to afford such inspectors right of entry in cases where there is reason to believe there is a contravention of this part of the Act. 90 Section 3 prohibits the reception of infants by certain persons under certain circumstances. Under Section 4, the local authority may fix the number of infants which may be retained in any dwelling. Section 5 requires the removal of infants kept: in overcrowded, dangerous, or insanitary premises; by drunken, immoral, or other undesirable person; or by any person or in any premises in contravention of the Act. Section 6 directs that notice shall be given to the Coroner of the death of any infant notified as being maintained for reward. Section 7 enacts that no person in charge of an infant coming within the terms of the Act shall have an insurable interest in such infant, and the attempt to effect insurance is made a punishable offence. Sections 8 and 9 relate to notices given and penalties that may be imposed upon offenders, from whose custody any infant is to be removed to a place of safety. With regard to the supervision of these nurse-children, the Select Committee on Infant Life Protection strongly advocated that, so far as possible, their inspection should be deprived of any rigid or formal official character, and certainly carried out by women. It was desirable, therefore, that Women Inspectors should be appointed for the purposes of the Act in the City, and on the advice of your Medical Officer, on the representation of the Sanitary and General Purposes Committees, the Court of Common Council transferred the administration of Part I. of the Children Act, relating to Infant Life Protection to the Sanitary Committee, who have at their disposal Women Sanitary Inspectors possessing special qualifications for appointment under the Act as regards Infant Life Protection. At a meeting of the Common Council on the 4th February, Miss A. J. Safford and Miss M. M. Pole were duly appointed, on the recommendation of the Sanitary Committee, Inspectors for the purpose of the Act, and the Committee authorised to take such steps as might be necessary to bring its provisions to the public notice. 91 NOTIFICATION OF BIRTHS ACT, 1907. An abstract of this Act was given in your Medical Officer of Health's Annual Report for 1907. The Act is adoptive at the discretion of any Local Authority in the United Kingdom. Its object is to ensure early notification of births to the Medical Officer of Health in order, when the circumstances appear to require it, that the mother may be advised in the nurture and rearing of the child in the earlier stages of its existence. It provides that in the case of every child born within the area, it shall be the duty of the father of the child, if he is actually residing in the house where the birth takes place at the time of its occurrence, and of any person in attendance upon the mother at the time of or within thirty-six hours of the birth, to give notice in writing of the birth to the Medical Officer of Health of the district in which the child is born. The Act also provides for the notification of still-births. This useful measure has been supplemented by the Children Act referred to on p. 89. Both these provisions are the result of the movement for the prevention of infantile mortality. The Notification of Births Act was formally adopted by the Corporation on 28th November, 1907, and having duly received the consent of the Local Government Board, came into force in the City on the 19th February, 1908. Lists of all notifications of births received by your Medical Officer are transmitted weekly to the London County Council in accordance with the provisions of Sub-section (5) of Section 2 of the Act in a form prescribed by the Local Government Board. When notification is made by a midwife, her name and address are also added, and care is taken to ascertain whether she is on the Midwives' Register. Copy of a circular letter sent on 4th February, 1908, to medical men and midwives practising in the City, is given in Appendix G, p. 209. During the whole year, 277 births were registered in the City, the parents being non-residents in 25 cases. Forty-nine of these births occurred in public Institutions in the City. In cases where the parents were non-residents, the births were reported to the Medical Officer of the district to which they belonged. Thirteen illegitimate births were registered during the year.* From the date of the Act coming into operation in the City, viz., 19th February, 1908, until the end of the year, 175 births of children whose parents resided in the City were notified, including six still-births. Forty-eight other notifications relating to children not belonging to the City were received during the same period and forwarded to the Medical Officers of their respective districts. * See footnote on p. 17. 92 Of the 175 City births, 138 were notified by Medical Men. 8 „ „ „ Parents. 23 „ „ „ Midwives. 6 ,, „ from Hospitals, Institutions, &c. In 50.9 per cent. of these, the notification was received within the Statutory limit of thirty-six hours, and the remaining 49.1 per cent. being sent at later periods. At the request of the Registrars of the two sub-districts in the City, a list of all notifications has been forwarded to them monthly. The routine visiting of infants has been conducted on the same lines as hitherto, except that in the case of those born since January, 1908, the three visits per case extending over the first 12 months of life have been, when necessary, increased to five during the first six months. The Woman Inspector engaged in making these visits has, almost without exception, been well received, and the mothers have expressed their willingness to supply any information dealing with the care of their children. Some cases have, unhappily, arisen when poverty has been the cause of the mother being physically incapable of nourishing her infant. In such circumstances, the attention of the Relieving Officer has been directed to the case, and, if necessary, letters of admission to Hospital obtained from the Justices sitting in the City, to whom I take this opportunity of conveying my thanks for their kindly offices in this respect. It should be mentioned that a considerable number of families have left the City during the year, and in cases where there was a child under one year of age, where possible, the Medical Officer of Health of the district into which the family removed has been notified. The same course has been adopted when infants born in the City have been put to nurse elsewhere. The mothers are, wherever possible, induced to suckle their children. The proportion of infants who have been breast fed for the first six months of life was 51 per cent. The first 10 months' working of the Act confirms what has long been more than suspected, viz., that there is a lack of accuracy in the registration of births, both as regards date and place, and it will be therefore a valuable check upon registration in those places where it has been adopted. 93 As a case in point, it was ascertained that in one instance a Jewish parent registered a birth as having occurred on the 19th August, whereas, as a fact, the child was born on the 6th May, as notified by the midwife who attended the parent. In another instance, a parent having business premises in the City and his home in Finsbury, registered the child as having been born in the City. There are some grounds for believing that many people are under the impression that children born in the City acquire thereby certain rights and privileges. I am informed that it is a common occurrence for persons born in the City, when making application for taking up the Freedom of the City at the Chamberlain's Court, to expect that they are entitled to it without any payment. Incorrect registrations, when discovered, were reported to the Registrars for the information of the Registrar-General. Delails are given on page 17 of mortality in the City of children under one year of age. The number of visits paid to infants during the year was 942. In the course of these visits, 47 sanitary defects found were remedied by the district Sanitary Inspectors. As it might be thought by some that the simplest method of ensuring early notification of births would have been to compel registration of births within five days, as is required in the case of deaths by the Births and Deaths Registration Acts, I submit the Registrar-General's opinion on the matter given before the Notification of Births Act was passed. "After serious consideration of the situation as it presents itself "to me, I have come to the conclusion that, whilst it would be unwise "at present to disturb the existing organisation of birth registration, "a well-devised system of early notification—legalized and worked in "conjunction with the present registration system on the one hand, "and with sanitary administration on the other—might, along with other "motive forces, serve as a most effective and lasting barrier with which "to stem the tide of infant mortality." From the experience already obtained in the working of the Notification of Births Act there is reason to believe this prediction will be in a great measure realized. 94 INDUSTRIAL EMPLOYMENT OF WOMEN. The Corporation, at the request of the Home Secretary, have undertaken to make an investigation into the industrial employment of women as far as it relates to the mothers of children born in the City, with a view of ascertaining whether there is sufficient justification for enforcing further restriction with regard to the employment of women in factories or workshops both before and after childbirth. The enquiry will extend over two years, and the results of the first year's investigation are now given. The proportion of women in industrial employment in the City during pregnancy is comparatively small, as many of them being wives of caretakers, are simply engaged in ordinary domestic duties, although in some cases where it is a part of their work to clean offices and many stairs, the work may be described as laborious. Further, there are many Jewish mothers residing in the City amongst whom it is unusual, after marriage, to do other than home work. Still-born 2 or 1.6 per cent. Premature births 3 ,, 2.5 „ Died before six mouths 7 „ 5.7 „ 96 TINNED FOODS. The alarm created in the public mind, owing to statements made in the Press and elsewhere as to the insanitary conditions prevailing in the canning factories in Chicago, and the seizure and condemnation of quantities of unsound tinned foods returned from South Africa after the war, has largely subsided, but the results of an investigation, conducted in the City on behalf of the Corporation, and of a further enquiry subsequently carried out in co-operation with the London Chamber of Commerce, showed there was great room for improvement in the methods of preparing tinned foods in other places than the United States of America. The circumstances were as follows: In June, 1906, I reported to the Sanitary Committee that, owing to the enormous quantities of tinned meat imported from abroad for consumption in this country, and the importance of the question both to the public health and to traders in the City, it was advisable to make a special investigation into the character of the tinned foods offered for sale to the public. The method of preserving food products in tins renders it difficult to rightly judge the nature and quality of the enclosed article, and no amount of inspection by external appearances can be sufficient to guarantee safety to the public health. "Blown" or damaged tins are readily detected, but the real danger lies in the difficulty of discovery of diseased or unsound meat after preservation. The only way in which the public can be adequately protected is to insist upon a strict inspection of the animals used, both before and after slaughter at the factory, coupled with the best sanitary surroundings through the whole process of manufacture. Thirteen samples of tinned meats from America were accordingly sent to Dr. Klein for bacteriological analysis, and 13 other samples of the same brands submitted to the Public Analyst, Dr. Teed, for chemical examination. The latter reported that all these samples were free from ptomaines, and that all except two (of which there was insufficient for examination) were free from boric acid and salicylic acid, and that no change had taken place in the constitution of the articles that would interfere with the analysis. The bacteriological examination showed that of these 13 samples, two were non-sterile—of which one was harmful to animals fed thereon. Five were sterile and proved themselves harmless to animals. Six were sterile, but injurious when eaten, as all the animals eating it, on being killed, showed signs of fatty degeneration of the liver, and the sample, although sterile and noninjurious. contained five human hairs (two of negro). 97 The results made it clear that some deleterious influence was exerted upon the animals subjected to the test in the six cases where the food was sterile, and in one case where it was non-sterile. In neither instance was any pathogenic organism identified, and Dr. Teed reported the absence of any ptomaines, boric and salicylic acids. The matter evidently required further investigation, and the Committee, at my suggestion, decided that further tests should be made, including British and Colonial goods as well as those from America. Forty-four samples were therefore obtained and submitted to Dr. Klein, who reported on them as under. Summarising the results of the bacteriological analysis, these samples may be grouped as follows :— "Group I. Samples which are sterile, and in mice caused no ill effects of any "kind. "Group II. Samples which are sterile, but caused in mice ill effects. "Group III. Samples which are not sterile, but caused in mice no ill effects. "Group I. comprises the majority of the samples analysed. Group II. could "have caused their ill effect, not owing to any microbe they contained—they "being sterile, but owing to some deleterious substance present in the material "before sterilisation, and which material was not affected by the subsequent "heating. Such are ptomaines and preservatives. "The samples of Group III. contained harmless microbes in the form of "spores, which are widely distributed in dust and in air, of which it is not known "whether, and under what conditions, they cause in animal food undesirable "changes. "As far as our present knowledge goes, they themselves do not cause "putrefactive changes, although they are occasionally found in association with "other more important microbes in decomposing materials. They themselves are "harmless to man and animals. "As has been mentioned in my Report, all the samples on opening were, to "the eye and nose, quite free from any change—fermentative and putrid—and "therefore the person opening them would not raise any objection to their being "fit for consuming. But the samples of Group II. would nevertheless be "objectionable on account of their ill effects, and those of Group III. would be "objectionable because not sterile. The principle that all preserved food should "be sterile is a good one, even if the microbes we find by our analyses are spores "and quite harmless; we do not know sufficient about their possible effects and "changes in course of long periods on the materials, such as are contained in "these tinned samples. (signed) "E. KLEIN." For convenience the results of the first series referred to in the foregoing are included With those of the second series. 98 The country of origin and number of samples examined were as under:— Prepared in Great Britain 12 „ ,, the Colonies 9 „ „ U.S.A. .34 „ „ South America 2 57 Of these the following 28, or only 49 per cent., were found to be in a wholly satisfactory condition, viz.:— Great Britain 6, or 50 per cent. Colonial 7 „ 77 „ U.S.A. 15 „ 44 „ South America None. 28 Summarising the results of the examination it was found that of the 57 samples— 28, or about 49 per cent, were good. 10 „ „ .18 „ were bad. 19 „ „ 33 „ were doubtful. The discovery of human hairs in four of the American preparations, and dirt and soot in another, is sufficient indication of a laxity of supervision in manufacture to give warrant for some of the charges made against the packing bouses in the United States. Dr. Klein's research showed clearly there was room for considerable improvement in the manner in which tinned meats were prepared both at home and abroad, and I advised the Committee that, having regard to the public health, it would be a wise precaution to institute further enquiries as to the quality of tinned food sold in the City. Both wholesale and retail traders in this country had already suffered seriously from the scare that had been created, and care had to be taken not to further prejudice them. In all cases where the results were found to be unsatisfactory, the retail firms were notified, and they withdrew the goods in question from sale. One English firm, being satisfied by their own experiments that their goods were unsatisfactory, ceased to manufacture, and the representatives in this country of foreign packers approached the Chamber of Commerce on the subject. This resulted in the Chamber endorsing an opinion expressed by their Canned Goods Trade Section that the Sanitary Committee should defer arriving at any decision until two or more men of the highest professional repute had examined and reported upon similar samples. 99 Upon the invitation of the Sanitary Committee a deputation of six members of the Chamber attended before the Committee to consider the correspondence that had passed in relation to this matter. Further correspondence ensued, and it was finally agreed that Dr. W. Bulloch, of the London Hospital, Professor R. T. Hewlett, of King's College, and Dr. Klein, of St. Bartholomew's Hospital, should make an investigation into a certain number of samples of tinned meat, the samples being taken by myself and supplied to the three bacteriologists. Samples were taken in triplicate, and examined independently by each analyst. Fifty samples were taken, which included all those found to be unsatisfactory in the previous examination, with the addition of certain others. In due course, the gentlemen engaged in this investigation presented their reports, copies of which were forwarded to the Chamber of Commerce, together with a copy of my interpretation of the results which were laid before the Chamber on the 7th March, 1907. Consideration of space prohibits their inclusion here, but it will be sufficient to say that the results in many cases were unsatisfactory, as all three investigators, two of whom had been selected by the Chamber of Commerce, who presumably represented the interests of the Home traders, reported that a considerable proportion of the samples were not sterile. The importance of this matter was directly pointed out to the representatives of the Chamber of Commerce at a Conference with the Sanitary Committee for the purpose of considering the reports, held at the Gnildhall on the 22nd March. On the 11th April, 1907, a copy of the following resolution was received from the Special Committee of the Canned Goods Trade Section of the Chamber of Commerce:— "That whilst believing that the sterilisation of Tinned Foods is desirable "whenever practicable, this Committee of the London Chamber of Commerce "is of opinion that the reports of the Bacteriological Experts employed to "examine samples of such foods are of so satisfactory a nature that there appears "no necessity for unduly alarming the public by publishing them, as on "account of their technical character there is always the risk of their being " misunderstood." It was impossible to accept this view of the matter, and in order that a clear understanding may be had of the situation, I submit the opinion expressed by the Experts referred to in the above resolution:— Dr. Klein.—"Although the microbes found in some of the samples were "few and of a harmless nature, it cannot be doubted that in course of time " physical and chemical changes might, and probably would, be caused by them, 100 "which would materially alter the character of the food, and make the latter "even harmful. "For these reasons I think it is most important that all preserved food intended " to be kept for an indefinite period should be absolutely sterile." Dr. Bulloch.—"In conclusion it is my opinion that all foods of the class "examined and intended to be kept if necessary for an indefinite period, should be "Sterile, and that sterilisation should be accomplished by heat alone, and in a " suitable vessel which can be hermetically sealed." Professor Hewlett.—"The B. subtilis and the B. mesentericus vulgatus seem to "be quite harmless organisms—guineapigs inoculated intraperitoneally and mice "fed with cultures being unaffected. Although this is the case, it must be a matter "of some concern that nearly two-thirds of the canned foods examined were not "sterile. If, for example, the sporing anaerobic Bacillus Botulinus of sausage "poisoning should by any chance be introduced into a sample, there is considerable "probability that it would survive and bring about changes in the food which would "result in the production of substances having a toxic action. It is of interest that "cocci and organisms of the B. coli and B. entertidis groups were not detected. "I hope also that I have made it clear in my Report that I view with " concern the fact that so many samples are not sterile. "I am in absolute agreement with Dr. Klein and Dr. Bulloch in considering that "these canned foods should be sterile." The verdict of these Experts must be accepted as final, and it only remained, lierefore, as a satisfactory solution of the difficulty, to induce the Chamber of "ommerce to fall into line with this view. Further correspondence ensued, and on the 14th May, 1907, a letter was eceived from the Chamber of Commerce enclosing a copy of the following ircular to Packers and Manufacturers of Tinned and Preserved Foods proposed 3 be issued by their Canned Trade Section. " London Chamber of Commerce, " lith May, 1907. "CANNED GOODS TRADE SECTION. "EXAMINATION OF TINNED FOODS. "In view of the action taken by the various Governmental and Local "Authorities in the United Kingdom, to enquire into the methods of manufacture "and packing of canned foods imported into this country, and their suitability "for human consumption, I am desired to state that in the opinion of the Canned "Goods Trade Section of the London Chamber of Commerce it is essential that "packers of such goods should adopt a process of manufacture which would result "in the complete sterilisation of those articles of food which, without detriment of u their nutritive value, can be so treated. "This opinion is expressed as a result of a careful bacteriological examination ot a large number of samples of such food, and as the outcome of the conferences "which have taken place between the Sanitary Committee of the Corporation of "London and this Chamber upon the subject." 101 This letter, while showing a desire on the part of the Chamber of Commerce to meet the wishes of the Sanitary Committee, fell short of the necessities of the situation. It was understood at the Conference held on the 22nd March, 1907, that there was a feeling that as sterilisation was not applicable to all classes of tinned goods equally alike, a schedule should be drawn up of those it was proposed to exempt. The Secretary of the Chamber of Commerce was therefore asked in a letter, dated 7th June, 1907, at the instance of the Sanitary Committee, to furnish them, for consideration at an early date, with a schedule of foods it was proposed to exempt, and with regard to other tinned articles that an undertaking be obtained from the Packers that they would adopt a process which would result in complete sterilisation. In reply thereto, in a letter dated 23rd July, 1907, the Committee was informed that the Canned Goods Section of the Chamber of Commerce had found it impracticable to make the Schedule of foods asked for, but that the packers would give every consideration to the Representatives of the Chamber. It was also stated that copies of the Reports of the three bacteriologists had been sent to the Local Government Board, who they considered should have it before them, for information only and not for publication, during the framing of any regulations the Board might make under the Public Health (Regulations as to Food) Act, 1907. In this connection I submit for consideration the general conclusion arrived at by Major W. W. O. Beveridge, D.S.O., and Captain H. B. Fawcus of the Royal Army Medical Corps, as the result of a series of exhaustive experiments with preserved meat, contained in their Report to the Sub-Committee on Food of the War Office Committee on the Physiological Effects of Food, Training and Clothing on the Soldier. (Journal of the Royal Army Medical Corps, March, 1908.) "(a) The bacillus under consideration, although non-pathogenic to animals, "decomposes tinned meats, and renders them quite unfit for consumption. There"fore processes of sterilisation of tins of meat must be used which will destroy the "spores of this bacillus. The optimum'temperature of growth of this bacillus is "blood heat (370 C.) 99° F. At this temperature inoculated tins of meat become "rapidly decomposed, but this decomposition is not necessarily made apparent at "once by the presence of gas in the tin. Blowing of the tins often does not take place within a fortnight, even at this temperature. Tins of meat contaminated uwith the spores of this bacillus could be kept at temperatures of 22° C. (72° F.) "and under for many months, without showing any signs of blowing. If, however, "such contaminated tins, although apparently sound when examined in this country, "were exposed to a temperature such as is likely to be met with in the Tropics, they "would rapidly become decomposed. 102 "(J) The rate of penetration of heat into the substance of the meat in tins during "sterilisation is extremely variable. We have based our conclusions on the average "of many experiments. To ensure complete sterilisation, the temperature of "the medium surrounding the tins must always be above 112° C. (234° F.). The "lowest temperature of the surrounding fluid which will completely sterilise "the tins within a reasonable time is 120° C. (248° F.), and this temperature must "act for not less than sixty minutes. From the two experiments recorded with a "fluid boiling at 130° C. (266° F.), it would appear that even then at least an hour "would be required to ensure the death of these very resistant spores. We are "aware that the above results do not tally with the usually accepted idea of tin " thermal death point of spore-bearing bacilli, but we would point out that experi"ments have not before, to our knowledge, been made with this particular bacillus. "The experiments were carried out with every available precaution against error "and repeated often enough to ensure accuracy." MARKING OF TINNED AND OTHER PRESERVED FOOD PRODUCTS. In June last I endeavoured to come to some arrangement with the New Zealand Authorities with regard to the marking of tinned foods in such a way as to indicate where and when they were packed. With this object in view the Chief Veterinarian to the New Zealand Government conferred with the Managers of some of the leading firms in New Zealand, who, while they pointed out some commercial objections to the idea as originally propounded, expressed themselves quite willing to do what was desired, provided it were done in such a manner as not to raise any objections on the part of the importers in this country. The following arrangement was suggested:— (1) That each tin bear a mark consisting of the initials of the packing company or individual, with perhaps the registered number of their premises (or the registered number alone, the New Zealand Veterinary Office supplying your Medical Officer with a list showing the premises to which each number applies). (2) That a cypher mark indicating approximately the date of packing be affixed to each tin. A schedule of cypher marks being sent to your Medical Officer. Each cypher mark to indicate a period of say two months—thus, one mark indicating January and February, 1909, the next mark March and April, 1909, and so on. In reply to a request to be furnished with our views hereon, I expressed my pleasure at hearing that the Managers of some of the leading firms in New Zealand realised the importance of the question, and were prepared to deal with it practically. 103 It was pointed out that the object was the protection of the public without interfering with commercial interests, and that it was clear that if a system of marking were adopted in any country such fact would be of great value as affecting a guarantee of age and condition. With regard to the first suggestion made with regard to a cypher mark indicating the names of the packers, I agreed this would meet the case. It is not necessary that the name of the packer should b e known to everyone, provided that the responsible authorities were in possession of the information, and a registered number or mark of some kind would well serve the purpose if a key were applied and kept up to date. As to suggestion (2) that the date should be indicated by a cypher, I was unable to concur, as in my view the figures of the year should be plainly shown upon the tin. If a cypher were used the information would be available for the protection of the trade only, while the object sought is to afford the purchaser as much protection as he is entitled to. There is a general consensus of opinion amongst health administrators that all tinned and potted meat and other food products should have the date or year of packing marked thereon, and it is to be hoped that the Local Government Board will bear this in mind when considering the framing of any regulation they may make with regard to this class of food under the provisions of the Public Health (Regulations to to Food) Act, 1907. In a Report by Dr. Buchanan on Tin in Canned Goods, dated 18th December, 1908, the following remarks are made: — "The enquiry illustrates the advantage which would be gained from the "administrative point of view if the person or firm by whom canned foods are "prepared were in a position to determine by marks, labels, or otherwise, the date "and place of preparation, and could be required to furnish such particulars, on "reason shown, to public health authorities concerned with food inspection and "wholesomeness of foods. Such a practice would have advantage from other points "of view, which need not here be gone into." 104 SODA WATER. In the course of an investigation into the cause of a case of diphtheria in May, 1907, it was found that suspicion attached to soda water, which the patient had been in the habit of purchasing from a City shop. A sample of this soda water was therefore taken and submitted to your Bacteriologist, who reported as follows:— "I received on May 2nd an unopened bottle of soda water for analysis qua " Diphtheria. "About 99 c.c. of the contents, after allowing to stand and to sediment, were "subjected to the centrifuge. With the deposit large plates and tubes were " inoculated and incubated at 37 deg. C. As a result the following was found: "The sediment contained an enormous number of microbes; they were (a) cocci, "(b) some sporing bacilli, and (c) the main mass were small grampositive non"mobile bacilli which in shape resembled diphtheroid microbes, but they gave no "Neisser staining, and injected in large doses into a guineapig's subcutaneous "tissue caused no change whatever, the animal developed no tumour and remained "quite normal. It is clear from these observations that the chief microbe present "in the sediment is not the Klebs-Loffler Diphtheria bacillus, although it most "probably—judged by the character and aspect of the colonies, the shape and "grampositive staining—belongs to the large tribe of 'diphtheroid bacilli.' "I was not a little surprised at the fact that this soda water contained such "abundance of living microbes capable of growing at body temperature. "The charging of the water with carbonic acid seemed to have left the water, "no doubt originally impure, in an unaltered state—unaltered, that is, as to its "abnormal bacterial nature." In consequence of the unsatisfactory condition of this sample, it seemed advisable that a general enquiry should be made into the character of the soda water sold throughout the City, and for this purpose thirty-six samples were collected from various shops and submitted to bacteriological analysis. The results of Dr. Klein's examinations may be shortly summarized as follows:— No. of Samples. Pure 10 or 27.7 per cent. Fairly pure 7 or 19.4 „ „ Impure 19 or 52.7 „ „ 36 These figures are sufficiently striking to call for further action with a view of effecting an improvement in the quality of soda water usually sold. 105 In all cases where impurity was found, the result was communicated to the manufacturers, and they at once expressed a desire to ascertain the cause of contamination and their intention of taking active steps to remove the same. A large number of factories were visited at the request of the owners. It was found, that in most cases the causes of contamination were defects in the system of bottle washing and exposure of water to risk of pollution in tanks constructed of wood (which it is practically impossible to keep at all times in a cleanly condition) and to the absence or imperfection of covers to the receptacles containing either the water or solution. In every case where the water was found to be unsatisfactory, some sufficient cause was found and pointed out to the manufacturers. The above facts were reported to the Sanitary Committee on the 24th June, and your Medical Officer of Health suggested that inasmuch as the makers were, up to this time, unaware of the condition of their water, and were anxious to do anything that was found necessary or advisable to improve the same, that rather than run the risk of seriously damaging an important industry, instead of at once publishing these results, he should be authorised to summon a conference of the trade and, after laying the position before them, ascertain their views as to the best course to adopt, both in their own and the public interest. This was agreed to, and a meeting of the Trade was held on the 18th July, 1907, which was attended by a large number of Mineral Water Manufacturers. A statement was made by your Medical Officer as to the result of the analyses, and the urgent need for immediate and effective action. A series of Regulations was also laid before the meeting which it wae suggested should be considered and adopted either in their entirety or with such modifications as might be suggested. SODA WATER FACTORIES. SUGGESTED REGULATIONS. Premises.—To be in good sanitary condition, Certificate from Medical Officer of Health, Sanitary Authority. Water.—Source of supply to be bacteriological ly examined at entrance to works. Filters.—If used, only Pasteur-Chamberland or Berkefeld filter. Storage Tanks.—To be of impervious material, viz., slate, glass or galvanized iron (not wood) and fitted with efficient, covers (glass or galvanized iron, with flanges) not wood. Tanks to be cleaned periodically. 106 Solution Tanks.—To be covered with impervious material (not wood) and cleaned after eachemptying. Bottle Washing.—All bottles to have preliminary soaking, and labels, &c., removed in separate tanks. Washing Tanks.—(1) If revolving trays are used, the water to be kept running in and out. Tanks to be of impervious material (not wood). The water to be entirely emptied, and the tanks cleansed several times during the day. The washing, brushing and rinsing tanks to be all fitted with an independent supply, water not to be allowed to run from one tank to another. The brushes used for cleaning require constant attention, and are to be at once changed if found defective or fouled. (2) If the washing tanks are filled with bottles, the water to be kept running, and the tank entirely emptied and cleansed after each charge of bottles. The Rinsing Jets.—To be of the skeleton pattern. Boards fitted to receive the bottles are nearly always dirty underneath. The jets require constant supervision to ensure their being efficient. The pressure to be sufficient to thoroughly cleanse bottles. Stoppers or Corks.—Not to be placed in the washing tanks. Bottles.—Not to be allowed to remain after washing. The water to keep pace with the filling, and bottles left over-night unfilled to be again rinsed before filling. Stoppers fitted with indiarubber rings to have these rings removed every time before washing. Stoppers, rings and corks to be washed in a stream of running water. All water used for washing bottles, corks, rings and stoppers, and for rinsing bottles to be of the same standard of purity as the water used for making the mineral water. Unless this be the case the purity of the finished article is ruined. Patent Ball Stoppered Bottles to have the rings removed before washing. (The shape of these bottles prevents proper cleaning). For safety only corked bottles should be used. (Signed) W. COLLINGRIDGE, Medical Officer of Health, City of London. Guildhall, 9th July, 1907. 107 The Conference, after a long discussion, agreed to accept the suggestions of your Medical Officer, and it was further decided that a Register be kept by the Mineral Water Trade Protection Society of those makers who would undertake to carry out these Regulations in their works, and who were prepared to obtain the certificates as to the sanitation of the premises and the bacteriological purity of their products. On the 17th September, 1907, in accordance with this decision, the following circular letter was issued by the Society to the trade. "The London Bottle Exchange and Mineral Water Trade Protection Society, Ltd., " Riidler Place, "Holland Street, "Blackfriars, "London, S.E., "September 17th, 1907. "To "As a consequence of the Report of the Medical Officer of Health of the "Corporation of the City of London, at a Conference with the Society held on "July 10th, it was Resolved : "'That this Meeting having carefully considered the recommendations of " 'Dr. Collingridge, agrees to carry out his recommendations as soon as possible,' "and this Resolution was subsequently affirmed by the Council, and the following "Resolution passed by the Council to carry out these arrangements: “ 'For purpose of Registration under the above, it is agreed that The London “ 'Bottle Exchange and Mineral Water Trade Protection Society, Limited, shall “ 'issue a Certificate to firms who are Members of the Society, showing that “ ' they have complied with the requirements of the Society.
