Yours faithfully, “W. DAVENPORT, “ Secretary.” “SODA WATER FACTORIES. “Regulations. “Water Supply.—The Water Supply used in the manufacture to be “bacteriologically examined. The standard of purity should not be lower than the “water supplied for drinking purposes in any district where the public water supply “is used. The authorities supplying such water will furnish free bacteriological “analysis of their water. “Analysis of Products.—Bacteriological analysis of the goods produced must “be furnished with the application for Registration. Bacteriologists have been “approved by the Medical Officer of Health of the City of London and by the “Society, and their certificates will be accepted. “Filters.—If the water is quite pure no filter is necessary, if impure no filter “is safe in practice. If filtration is considered advisable only special filters as “approved by the Medical Officer of Health should be used. “Rings and Stoppers.—The Medical Officer of Health of the City of London “calls attention to the impossibility of properly cleaning stoppers or patent bottles “having rings inserted in the bottles unless such rings are removed. In deference “however to the general views of the trade, and inasmuch as stoppers are used to “meet the demand of the public, he agrees that it will be sufficient if all bottles “containing rings and stoppers and rings and stoppers when separated from the “bottle are soaked in a solution of bi-sulphate of lime, (1 in 250, say¾ oz. to 1 gallon “of water), and before using washed in a stream of running water. 109 “Storage Tanks.—Storage Tanks should be constructed of some impervious “material, such as slate, glass, porcelain, galvanized iron or copper, not wood, and ''fitted with efficient covers, also of impervious material not wood. Tanks should “be cleaned at stated periods. “Solution Tanks.—Solution Tanks should be constructed of some impervious “material, such as slate, glass, porcelain, galvanized iron or copper, not wood, and “fitted with efficient covers, also of similar impervious material, not wood, and “cleaned after each emptying. “Bottle Washing.—All bottles to have preliminary soaking, and labels, &c., “removed before placing in washing tanks. This preliminary soaking may be “carried out in tanks of wood or other suitable material. “Washing Tanks.—If revolving trays are used, the water to be kept running “through. Washing Tanks to be of impervious material, viz.: slate, glass, “porcelain, galvanized iron or copper, not wood. The water to be entirely emptied “and the tanks cleansed several times during the day. “The washing, brushing and rinsing tanks to be all fitted with an independent “supply, water not to be allowed to run from one tank to another. The brushes “used for cleaning require constant attention, and are to be at once changed if “found defective or fouled. “It is most important that all water used for washing bottles, corks, rings and “stoppers, and for rinsing bottles, should be of the same standard of purity as the “water used for making the Mineral water. Unless this be the case the purity of “the finished article is uncertain.“ The result of this circular letter has been most encouraging. At the end of the year 1908, no less than 45 firms had registered with the Society, and others have announced their intention of doing so. The advantage of a Trade managing its own affairs requires no emphasizing, and the prompt response to the appeal is distinct evidence of the good faith and public spirit of the manufacturers. The effect has been that they have, in the interests of the Public Health as well as their own, voluntarily put themselves to very great expense in order to guarantee as pure a supply as is practically possible. The fact that this has been done should convey to the public a confidence in the purity of Mineral Waters which could be obtained in no other way. It is proposed to keep up the standard by systematic inspection, and publication of the names of those registered will be made by the Society. As to those who have not yet come into line, they will be dealt with in the usual way, samples being taken from time to time, and submitted for analysis, such action as is necessary being taken against the Vendors of any found to be impure. 110 ILLNESS FROM EATING CONTAMINATED FOOD. Two cases of illness occurred during the year in which the eating of questionable food was suspected of being the cause. The first was reported on the 10th January, 1908, when a customer, who had partaken of a meat pie at a licensed house in the City, was shortly after attacked with illness which necessitated his seeing a medical man. The unconsumed portion of the pie, which had been retained by the customer and brought by him to Guildhall, showed green colouration over the whole of the crust, penetrating to a depth of a quarter of an inch. This was submitted to your Public Analyst for examination, who reported that it contained .0053 per cent, of copper. The total amount of sulphate of copper present was .303 grains. Assuming that the pies were all approximately of the same weight as determined by weighing others, viz., 5¾ ounces, and that the copper was distributed uniformly over the undercrust, as was the case with the sample analysed, this is equivalent to *521 grains of sulphate of copper per pie, equal to 1.45 grains per pound. Every facility was rendered by the proprietor of the establishment where the pie was sold in an endeavour to ascertain how the copper gained access to it, but without result. The pies were made on the premises; the sample was one of a number eaten the same day, and was the only one of which any complaint was made. The premises were well kept, and a careful investigation, especially with regard to copper utensils used in cooking, failed to show any reason for the presence of the copper in the pie. It was noted that the pie submitted for examination was staler than the one unsold we found on the premises. No other case of the kind was reported. On the 21st February, 1908, a customer who had suffered severely after eating a fish rissole purchased in the City, was referred to me by the City Police. Samples of the food were submitted to Dr. Klein, who reported that it followed from his analysis and experiments that the material possessed no pathogenicity for the guineapig, but had all the bacterial characters of being polluted with filth. All the microbes found in the material were non-sporing, and therefore devitalized by a temperature of 70° C and over, and these results proved that the interior of the material could not have been heated to that temperature. Upon enquiry at the establishment where the food in question was prepared and sold, it was found that the fish employed in making rissoles was of the best quality, and the arrangements for the preparation and cooking left nothing to be desired. Ill This was an isolated case, no others being reported. It appeared therefore the cause of the evil was purely accidental. Although the cause of the mischief was not determined in either of the above cases, they illustrated the importance of ensuring that food should always be prepared under proper hygienic conditions. A systematic inspection of the kitchens and places where food is prepared in restaurants, &c., was instituted in 1902, when 155 were visited and placed on the register of workplaces. At the end of 1908 there were 839 of these places registered, viz.: 628 situated in restaurants, and 211 in tea rooms. 112 CONTAMINATED SHELLFISH. MUSSELS. On the 9th October, 1908, communications were received from the Medical Officers of Health of both Islington and Wandsworth relating to cases of enteric fever occurring in their districts, attributed in each instance to the patients having eaten mussels procured from salesmen in Billingsgate Market. The names were given of three dealers from whom the supplies in question were obtained, but in no case were they able to identify with certainty the source of origin of the mussels after a lapse of four weeks. Each of these firms dealt in Dutch mussels, and could produce certificates of the Netherland Government that their consignments came from unpolluted sources. One salesman suggested the possibility of South Dutch mussels being the produce of more than one firm, but sent in one consignment, and undertook in future to apply for certificates from each Dutch firm to whom they made payments. On the 23rd October I was informed of another case of enteric fever at Islington, in which mussels purchased from one of the salesman referred to in the previous case was supposed to be implicated, but upon enquiry it was found that this dealer had not supplied mussels to the local trader in Islington. On the 23rd November intimation was received that a woman, aged 36, residing in Wandsworth, who had eaten mussels procured from Billingsgate Market on the 5th November, was suffering from enteric fever, the first symptoms of the disease having appeared on the 14th November. It was found upon enquiry that the mussels in question were imported from Holland. A sample of these, marked (1), was taken at Billingsgate Market on the 23rd November, and submitted to Dr. Klein for analysis, who reported thereon as under:— “30th November, 1908. “I received, on 23rd November, a sample of mussels (South Dutch) which are “supposed to be similar to some that were connected with a case of enteric fever. “These mussels were dealt with in the following manner:— “After opening and draining the liquor, the bodies only of 12 mussels were cut “up finely in a sterile dish; the fluid resulting from this mince was turbid, and was “used for making the following cultures. I ought to state that the total amount of “this fluid was 18 c.c., and that each culture was charged with 1/10 c.c., that is, “1/15th part of the body of a mussel:— “(a). Surface of two large plates with Drigalski-Conradi medium. “(b) Two tubes containing Malachite green bile broth—mal. green, 1 :1,500. “(c) Two tubes containing Malachite green bile broth—mal. green, 1 : 1,600. 113 “The result of these cultures was as follows :— “(a) Drigalski plates—large number of colonies of the B. coli communis “type, calculated to amount to over 300 per body of one mussel; large numbers “of colonies of streptococci; few blue colonies, in general aspect not unlike “colonies of B. typhosus. “These blue colonies were sub-cultured in the usual media by which “B. typhosus is investigated ; agglutination tests with typhoid blood serum was “also made. The result was negative qua B. typhosus. “(b) Both Malachite green broth tubes, 1 : 1,500, were free of growth. “(c) Both Malachite green broth tubes had slight growth of cylindrical, very “motile bacilli. “These last tubes were used for sub-culture for further Drigalski plates; for “gelatine culture and for agglutination tests, with the result that the above bacilli “were not those of B. typhosus.“ These mussels were consigned to a firm of fish salesmen in the Market from a dealer at Bruinisse, in Holland, arriving vid the Batavia line of steamships. They were accompanied by a certificate, of which the following is a copy:— “KINGDOM OF THE NETHERLANDS. “1908. “Valid only for the sending. “d.d. 21 November, 1908. “Certificate. “The Board of Fisheries on the Scheldt and Zealand streams after personal “investigation and personal knowledge, and also on the basis of the topographical “and bacillary investigations, carried on by its orders for considerable time at “fixed periods, both of the water and of the oysters/mussels, certifies that the “ponds and the breeding grounds of Mr. Jan Johns Wemmers, Bruinisse, situated “within the district of the Scheldt and Zealand streams, are free from noxious “microbes, and in consequence of their position, not exposed to any reasonable risk “of pollution by sewage, or objectionable filth. “The Board aforesaid. PRESIDENT LS “(Signatures of) and SECRETARY-TREASURER.” In view of the results of Dr. Klein's analysis, which showed that, although he was unable to identify the actual bacillus of enteric fever, the mussels were polluted with organisms of a fæcal character, a copy of his Report was forwarded to the Consul-General for the Netherlands, with the suggestion that it would be advisable that the local conditions at Bruinisse should be further investigated. 114 On the 25th November notification was received that a male, aged 28, residing in the Wandsworth district, who had eaten mussels supplied to a local dealer by a salesman in Billingsgate Market, was suffering from enteric fever, the first symptoms of the disease being observed on the 10th November. • In this instance the mussels had been procured from South Devonshire. A sample from this source was obtained, and sent to Dr. Klein for examination, -who reported as follows:— “7th December, 1908. “lie Mussels No. 2. Received November 30th.—They were analysed with a “view to detecting microbes belonging to the Gaertner typhoid group. “The bodies of 12 mussels were finely minced in a sterile dish ; the fluid “resulting from the 12 bodies amounted to about 18 c.c. In all instances the “fluid was turbid, and was used in amounts of 1/10 c.c. for each of two large plates “with Drigalski-Conradi medium and for each cf two tubes of Malachite green bile “salt broth. The result of these cultures and numerous sub-cultures was as “follows:— “Fifteen B. coli per mussel. “No B. Gaertner. No B. typhosus.” Upon enquiry it was found that the mussels in this case came from Lympston, South Devonshire, situated in the estuary of the River Exe. This place had been under the supervision of the Fishmongers' Company, and arrangements made for mussels to be gathered from an approved shoal, and no longer taken from a particular place, as in former times, that was considered an undesirable source of supply. As it was evident from Dr. Klein's Report that these instructions had been disregarded, strict instructions were given that in no case were mussels to be sent to market from places not approved by the Fishmongers' Company. I had occasion, in 1903, to direct attention to the danger incurred in the consumption of mussels derived from polluted sources. In the course of that year, out of 11 samples taken in Billingsgate Market and neighbourhood, no less than 10 were found to be sewage contaminated. Seven of the polluted samples having been imported from Holland, the Fishmongers' Company co-operated with the Netherlands Government with a view to a thorough investigation being made of the conditions under which they were cultivated and exported to this country. At the present time it is the custom of the wholesale dealers in the City, who purchase shellfish from Holland, to see that each consignment is accompanied by an official certificate issued by the Netherlands authorities to the effect that the produce is derived from unpolluted sources, but, in spite of these precautions, polluted samples, as shown above, are occasionally found. 115 Cases of mussel poisoning are common during the mussel season, the symptoms of the disease being disturbances of the gastro-intestinal and peripheral nervous systems. There is also very strong evidence that they may be an active agent in the spread of enteric fever. Dr. Bulstrode, in his valuable and exhaustive Report to the Local Government Board in 1896, quotes several instances where mussels have come under suspicion as having conveyed enteric fever. Recently (July, 1906), Dr. Robertson, Medical Officer of Health for Birmingham, has published a list of 74 cases coming under his notice where there appeared to be some connection between the consumption of mussels and disease. In 62 of these cases the onset of the disease occurred daring the height of the mussel season. In two notable instances mussels were eaten raw, and in each of these the consumer was attacked with typhoid fever. OYSTERS. A case of enteric fever in which oysters were supposed to be implicated was reported on the 30th November. The patient was attacked with the disease on the 16th November. He had attended public dinners in the City both on the 28th October and the 9th November, and it is believed partook of oysters on each occasion. If oysters were the cause of the illness the patient most likely became infected on the earlier of these dates, viz., 28th October. A sample of the oysters supplied was procured and submitted to Dr. Klein, who reported as follows:— “7th December, 1908. “Re Oysters No. 1. Received November 30th.—These were analysed with a view “to detecting microbes belonging to the Gaertner group. “Six oysters received. After draining the liquor the bodies of these oysters “were received in sterile dish and finely minced. The fluid resulting from these “six bodies amounted to about 16 c.c. The fluid was turbid, and 1/10 c.c. was used “for each of the following cultures, viz.: two large plates with Drigalski-Conradi “medium, and two tubes of Malachite green bile salt broth. “ The results o£ those cultures and numerous sub-cultures was as follows:— “ No B. Coli. No B. Gaertner, and No B. typhosus." These results show that, assuming the sample examined was derived from the same source as those eaten by the patient on the 28th October, the oysters in question were not connected with the illness. A case of enteric fever, attributed to the consumption of oysters at a City luncheon-bar, was reported from Wood Green on the 27th November, 1908. 116 In this case the oysters in question came from the neighbourhood of Whitstable. A sample, stated to have been derived from the same source as those suspected, was submitted to Dr. Klein, who reported thereon as under:— “ 7 th December, 1908. “ Oysters. No. 2. Received 30th November, 1908.—Six oysters received. Treated “ in the same manner as those in the previous case. (Oysters, Sample No. 1) gave “ the following results :— “ 16 B. coli per oyster. No B. Gaertner. No B. typhosus.“ This evidence was sufficient to show that there was no causal relation between the oysters and the disease. During December, 1908, the Medical Officers of Health of the Boroughs of Southwark, Paddington and Wandsworth respectively, were engaged in enquiries with regard to cases of enteric fever, when in each instance oysters supplied by a particular firm in the City were suspected. This dealer first came under my notice in June, 1903, when oysters sold by him were found to be polluted, and an order made at the Mansion House for their destruction. In November, 1906, in investigating a case of enteric fever at Walthamstow he was found selling “ oysters from his own beds,“ which proved to be highly polluted. Dutch oysters sold by him at that time were found to be grossly polluted. On the 8th December, 1906, he gave an undertaking to discontinue selling oysters from certain places until permitted to do so by the Fishmongers' Company, and on the 11th of that month it was ascertained that he had in the past purchased supplies from a company that had been in the habit of washing their oysters in a polluted pit. It was exceedingly difficult to secure any direct evidence with regard to where this man had purchased his supplies in the case brought to my notice in December last, as little or no reliance could be placed upon his statements. A sample taken at his shop in the City by an Inspector on the 15th December was stated by him in the forenoon to have come “ from their own beds,“ while in the afternoon he said they came from Holland. Dr. Klein's report on this sample was as under :— “ 21s£ December, 1908. “ Oysters. No. 3. Received 15th December.—The analysis of these oysters was “ made with ten oysters ; after draining the liquor, the bodies of these were finely “ minced, the resulting turbid fluid was used for cultures, with a view (a) of “ testing the condition of the oysters as to pollution in general, and (b) as to “ specific pollution with either the B. typhosus or with microbes belonging to the “ Gaertner-Paratyphoid group. 117 “As to (a)—The oysters showed no evidence of serious general pollution. The “number of B. coli calculated per one whole oyster was less than 8 (eight). There “were no Enteritidis spores per one whole oyster. “As to (b).—Two large Drigalski plates, each infected with 1/10th part of a whole “oyster, showed no colonies which in aspect, constitution and sub-cultures corre“sponded to either the B. typhosus or to the Gaertner-Paratyphoid group." These results indicate the oysters came from a source of doubtful purity, and the Fishmongers' Company, at my request, undertook to keep me advised from time to time as to where this dealer obtained his supplies in future. As regards the general question of shellfish pollution, it is greatly to be regretted that in spite of the opinion expressed by the Commissioners in their Fourth Report on sewage treatment and disposal, issued in December, 1903, that there is a growing demand for legislation dealing with the matter, and that “the evil is sufficiently grave to demand a remedy” no action has been taken by the Government for giving effect to the recommendations of the Commissioners. The following remedial measures were advocated in the Report referred to:— 1. Purification of sewage before its discharge in tidal waters. 2. The seizure and destruction of unwholesome shellfish exposed for sale. 3. The setting up of a competent authority to deal with all tidal waters, with power to control foreshores, to close dangerous beds, pits, ponds and layings, and in certain circumstances to require treatment of sewage. In my opinion, No. 3 is of the greatest importance, and until such competent authority, armed with ample powers to give effect to their views, is established, the present unsatisfactory state of affairs will continue. 118 DAIRIES, COWSHEDS AND MILK SHOPS ORDERS. The additional powers conferred upon Sanitary Authorities in the Metropolis by the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1908, include the following:— Section 5 enacts that any Sanitary Authority may remove from or refuse to enter on the Register the names of dairymen or purveyors of milk whose premises are. in the opinion of such Sanitary Authority, for any reason unsuitable for the sale of milk thereon. Any person feeling aggrieved by any decision of a Sanitary Authority under the provisions of this section has the right of appeal within twenty-one days from the date of such decision to a Court of Summary Jurisdiction. Hitherto purveyors of milk could be placed on the Register without any restrictions, and it is greatly to the advantage of the public health that Sanitary Authorities should be armed with the powers provided by this section as the sale of milk in small chandlers' shops, entirely unfitted for the proper storage of such a susceptible article as milk, is most objectionable from a public health point of view. The action taken in the course of the year under the section referred to above, with regard to those premises in the City unfitted for the sale or storage of milk, is referred to on page 67. At the end of the year there were 612 purveyors of milk registered in the City of London, as compared with 606 in 1907, an increase of 6. During the year the sale of milk was discontinued at 42 premises, and the owners' names removed from the register, 48 new premises were added, and 41 changes of ownership necessitating re-registration occurred. Each place on the register is regularly and systematically inspected, and their general condition is now much better than obtained in 1903, when the revised regulations for milkshops in the City came into force. The omission to re-close the lids of milk pans after serving a customer is still far too prevalent, 42 cases being reported in the course of the year; cautionary letters were sent to the offenders in each instance. Three cases of Infectious Disease (all Scarlet Fever) occurred at registered premises during the year, the necessary cleansing, disinfection, and usual precautionary measures being carried out in each instance. A list of registered purveyors of milk in the City is given in Appendix D, p. 180. 119 ICE CREAMS. Under the provisions of the Ice Cream Sections, Nos. 42, 43 and 44 of the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1902, which became operative on 1st November, 1903, any person engaged in the manufacture, sale or storage of ice cream, or any such commodity, who does any act or thing likely to expose such commodity to infection or contamination, or omits to take any proper precaution for the due protection of such commodity from infection or contamination, shall be liable for every such offence, on conviction, to a penalty not exceeding forty shillings. He is also liable to a similar penalty if he omits to notify the outbreak of any infectious disease amongst persons employed by him in the sale, manufacture or storage of such commodity. An itinerant vendor of ice cream, &c., must exhibit a notice on his barrow stating the name and address of the person from whom he obtains such articles, failing which, he also incurs a penalty not exceeding forty shillings. In 1908 there were 246 premises in the City where ice creams were sold, as compared with 207 in 1907, and 233 in 1906. These places may be classified as under:— Place of Manufacture. Number. In the City. In other districts. Tea shops 123 40 83 Confectioners 20 16 4 Small confectioners and light . ment shops 103 53 50 Total 246 109 137 Of the 103 small confectioners, 101 are foreigners. The number of places in the City where ice creams are manufactured increased from 57 in 1907, to 109 last year, while 137 dealers obtained their supplies from other districts, as compared with 150 in 1908. From enquiries made in other districts, it was ascertained that in all but nine cases the manufacture was carried on under satisfactory sanitary conditions, and in each of the cases reported against the purchase of ice cream from them was prohibited until notification was received that the necessary sanitary improvement had been effected. Itinerant Ice Cream Vendors. In the course of the year 41 itinerant ice cream vendors were found in the City, an increase of one over last year. It was ascertained that in all cases the material they were selling was made outside the City. 120 Upon making enquiries of authorities of outside districts it was found that in six cases the addresses displayed on the barrows were incorrect, one man had changed his residence, and that in one instance the ice cream was made under unsatisfactory conditions. As the police have no power of making arrests in cases where no notice of place of manufacture is displayed, there will always be a difficulty in exercising control over these itinerant dealers. The following prosecutions were instituted during the year for non-compliance with the law with regard to exhibiting notices on their barrows stating the names and addresses of the persons from whom the ice cream was obtained:— Mortomeo de Genaro, 5, Back Hill, Saffron Hill 5s. and 3s. expenses. Frangeris Ericono, 51, Ivy Street, Hoxton 2s. ,, 3s. „ Antonio Stechetto, 7, Back Hill, Saffron Hill 2s. „ 3s. „ Guiseppe Pacini, 39, Warner Street, Clerkenwell 10s. „ 10s. 6d. „ 121 MEAT SUPPLIES IN 1908.* It is unpleasant when contemplating the deliveries of meat to the Central Market again to have to deal with a falling-of, still more so when comparing 1908 with previous years to find that some eight years of more or less steady progress have been obliterated, and that we are now in about the same place as we were in 1900. This question of decreasing market deliveries has been the subject of comment in these reports on previous occasions, and it has been pointed out that the establishment of dep6ts at different centres in the outer suburbs of London must inevitably detract from the amount of trade here; the worst feature of it is that these dep6ts are worked by firms who, though they cannot be said to constitute a ring or trust, have working arrangements which are quite as effectual as any organisation of the kind could be. We propose classifying the supplies in the manner adopted last year. 1. English Town-Killed.—This consists largely of beef from the Metropolitan Cattle Market, Islington, pigs consigned to or purchased by different slaughterers, and after dressing marketed at Smithfield ; sheep, lambs and calves purchased at various markets, and, when dressed, sent here to be sold. The opening months of the year were favourable to the cattle trade, supplies from abroad were light, and the fact that no United States cattle would be sent unless they realised a price at which the English grazier could also produce beef, was made the most of for a time, the sums obtained must have been satisfactory to the sellers ; but the weather became treacherous, half fat cattle were pushed out, and when once the all-important factor of quality was neglected the inevitable slump came, because the home productions were no longer competing with United States bullocks of prime quality, but with the low-priced refrigerated meat of Argentina. This condition of things lasted nearly to Christmas, then foot-and-mouth disease broke out in several States of America, and some of the principal ports for the shipment of cattle were closed. With the Americans competing less keenly prices again rose, consequently cattle good enough for the Christmas trade realised sums which have not been heard of for a long time. Of sheep there is almost every Monday a good show at Islington, and for the first half of the year they sold well, but later, when it appeared likely that the turnip crop in the middle and southern portions of England would be very light, if not an utter failure, a large number of half-meated sheep and lambs came on the market, and a little later plenty of excellent Dutch mutton, for which the owners were willing to accept very low prices, was sent, the result being that the later months of the year have seen a level of prices most unsatisfactory to the British sheep farmer. It would be improper to close our remarks on this section without some reference to the improved conditions under which slaughtering is now carried on at the Islington Cattle Market. A long range of slaughterhouses has been built, lined with sanitary bricks, and provided with the necessary appliances * i am indebted to Mr. G. P. Terrett for the remarks on pp. 121-125. 122 « for flushing and washing the walls; also with electric hoists for raising the cattle in the course of dressing, and an elaborate system of drainage. In close proximity, and connected by elevated rails along which the sides of beef are conducted, is an extensive well-ventilated cooling shed, constructed with light from the north, so as to exclude, as far as possible, the rays of the sun. A pig slaughterhouse, fitted in the manner most approved by modern experts, and a large cooling shed for the dressed carcasses of pigs, is provided; and also a Podewil apparatus for the destruction of condemned meat, slaughterhouse refuse, &c. Probably experience will suggest some details in which further improvements may yet be made in any case it is a laudable attempt to keep pace with the times, and a great improvement on the dark, dirty, insanitary places which until now have done duty as slaughterhouses at the first Cattle Market in England. 2. British Country-Killed.—Taken altogether this is the finest meat the Londoner gets. The trade in Scotch beef has been affected by the conditions previously mentioned, but a great deal of very excellent beef has been sent from the Aberdeenshire districts. There has also been an abundance of both English and Scotch mutton throughout the year, the quality of which is unsurpassed in any market in the world. The prevalence of tuberculosis has militated against any great development of the home-grown pig trade; and the laxity of inspection at some other centres has diverted supplies away from London. Taking these two sections together, it is encouraging to find that in spite of adverse conditions prevailing during the late summer and autumn months the home producer has more than held his own, and that a very creditable increase in the tonnage of British meat has found its way to the Central Markets. 3. Foreign-Killed., i.e., Continental, chiefly from Holland, Denmark and Belgium.—There was a moderate supply of Dutch mutton in the early months of the year, but during the fall there has been a very large quantity of which the quality has for the most part been excellent; it has the effect of "knocking the bottom out of the market," i.e., of depressing prices to a level at which they are no longer remunerative to the home producer. Of pork there has also been a fair quantity, but in this respect there has not been the tendency to swamp the market so observable with the mutton. A Netherland Royal decree was issued on June 24th making it compulsory that all meat sent from Holland to the United Kingdom should be inspected, and none except that which bore the label affixed by the Inspectors should be allowed for shipment ; this, we were informed, was to come into operation on January 1st, 1909, but late in December the Authorities suddenly altered their minds, and deferred the date at which it would become effective ; probably the same effect can be achieved by the regulations drawn up by our own Local Government Board, but that does not excuse the vacillation of the Dutch Government. More stringent regulations, combined with a system of meat inspection on an altogether wider basis, have been 123 promulgated by the Danish Government, and, if the system works out in practice as well as it looks on paper, there ought to be no more complaints as to the soundness of meat imported from that country. This consists of beef, of which the quality is "middling" to "fair"; mutton, some of which is indifferent, but the greater part "very useful"; and pork of varying size and quality, with carcasses of veal generally too large to be graded "first class," but of undoubted soundness. Belgium has remained in the same unsatisfactory position; there may be some sort of inspection at the place of slaughter, but if so, it is of so nebulous and uncertain a character, that for all practical purposes we on this side must regard all Belgium products as "not inspected." 4. Cattle Imported Alive and Slaughtered at Deptford and Elsewhere.—This is a branch of the trade which by its very nature could not fail to make itself attractive to carcass butchers. A man requiring 50 or 100 bullocks finds them classified and graded to suit the particular branch of the trade which he has made it his study to cultivate, and the purchase of such a lot is effected at one transaction without the loss of time involved amongst home-grown stock in picking them up in two's and three's from a number of separate sellers ; then in the provision of slaughterhouses, cooling sheds, refrigerators, &c., the Corporation has done everything in its power to foster and encourage business, with the result that while the British Cattle Market languishes, the one devoted to Foreign trade thrives. For a brief period during the summer there was a check, and another again at Christmas. The latter of these was attributable to an outbreak of foot-andmouth disease ; the first was stated to have arisen from the financial panic which so upset the United States, or it may have been an attempt on the part of the cattle dealers to force up prices ; it is, however, probable that the real cause will be found where it is likely to recur with increasing frequency, viz., in a genuine scarcity of beef. The requirements of America for home consumption are greater now than ever, while owing to the land being taken up for building railways and other purposes, the sources of supply are constantly being pushed further West, which means increased cost in reaching the seaboard ; consequently, those who want low-priced beef must look elsewhere than to America for it. Of the cattle which Canada has sent, those from the Eastern States have shown some improvement in quality and condition, while those from Manitoba and the Western portions of the Dominion are imperfectly made up, of irregular age and very wild, the natural result of having passed all their lives on the ranches ; yet they furnish a lot of good food at a moderate price. Argentine cattle are still prohibited from coming alive, a fact which is in some respects to be regretted, as the sale and slaughter of them, with the conversion of the hides, fats and offals into commercial products would afford work for a great many men, while the heads, livers, &c., would be useful, cheap food for the poorer classes. The length of time which has elapsed since any outbreak of foot-and-mouth disease in Argentina seems to reduce the risk almost to vanishing point. Very few sheep have come, and those few of indifferent quality. The importation of Argentine sheep to Antwerp, where they are slaughtered for the 124 London Market, continues, and in this connection it is interesting to note that a large number of Argentine store sheep are bought by Belgian farmers to fatten, so far as we can learn, without any bad results such as carrying footand-mouth disease. 5. Refrigerated Beef.—The best of this comes from the United States, and is generally of excellent quality; for many years this had been relied upon as furnishing good and cheap food for the masses, but home requirements have grown, and good Chicago beef can only be sent here if there is something like a certainty of it realising a fairly high price; consequently, the quantity has been getting smaller, while the Argentine coming in increasing volume has precluded the possibility of any such rise in price as would make larger shipments from the United States profitable. Concerning the Argentine, we should like to say a good word. Most of the works are in the hands of British firms (though two large ones have passed to Americans who market the produce in England), the trade is financed by British capitalists, the cattle and sheep have been bred from stock imported from Great Britain, and it seems likely that Argentina will play an important part in the meat supply of this country for many years. Much of the beef has been better made up for slaughter, and it is most interesting to note that if a cargo is out of condition, it is generally a poor lot of beef; if a few quarters amongst a lot are out of condition, those are almost invariably the weakest and thinest; hence it seems fair to argue that if the beef is to arrive in good condition it must be beef not lacking in substance to begin with. Such, indeed, is our experience of refrigerated meat throughout, as, for instance, every attempt to bring mutton at the comparatively high temperature of refrigerated beef has been a failure simply because the carcass of mutton was of insufficient bulk and thickness to remain rigid during the process of refrigeration. If we might, without offence, give the meat traders a word of advice, it would be "Look well to the Argentine meat supply, and take care that it does not get monopolised by foreign speculators; then improve the quality of the goods shipped by careful selection, by proper storage during the voyage, and by rapid distribution on its arrival here." Some reference was made last year to the Linley system of preserving beef in sterilized air. Several vessels have been fitted up with the necessary apparatus, and whenever it has been well selected and properly handled, it has arrived in a condition which argues that the system has many advantages, but whenever beef of indifferent quality has been shipped, it is too much to expect that any method of preservation will make it into desirable meat. 6. Frozen Meat from New Zealand, Argentina and Australia—has been of good quality throughout the year, the number of thin, wasted and unsaleable carcasses being less than in any previous year. The principal novelty was a small shipment of lambs from Natal, which arrived early in the year, and of which there has been no repetition; they were of fair quality, though carrying abundant evidence that the flock from which they were taken had not been subjected to the many years of careful selection necessary to 125 eliminate all undesirable features. A general summary of the trade will show that the Argentine shippers have again been very aggressive, both with beef and mutton, with the effect that Australian products are driven quite into the background, and the priority of New Zealand in point of quality is seriously menaced. So far, but little lamb has been sent from Argentina. New Zealand graziers, on the contrary, have made a special study of it, and have succeeded in producing an article which meets with the highest approbation here, though merchants are prone to keep them too long in the refrigerator, whereby the quality is impaired. Australian lambs have shown a satisfactory improvement in quality and preparation. The "abominations " in the shape of boneless beef and other scrap meats which have been so freely inflicted upon us for the last few years, have been less in evidence as the year drew towards a close, probably as a result of the action taken by the Local Government Board. They have never been very popular amongst Londoners, who evince no partiality for confidence tricks being played upon them in respect of their food. Early in the year, one Inspector (W. E. Down), fell ill, and has been unable to resume his duties* ; this difficulty has been met by the employment of an Assistant Inspector on one section of the Market, and by the addition of a youth, who sees to the work of collection and at the same time acquires the knowledge necessary for responsible work. The following Naval Officers have received instruction in the examination of meat, viz. : one Deputy Store Officer, eight Fleet Paymasters, seven Staff Paymasters, five Paymasters, nine Assistant Paymasters, and one Paymaster of the Cape Royal Naval Reserve, to all of whom Certificates of competency have been issued. Five persons have been summoned for sending diseased meat to the Central Markets, and convictions obtained in four cases. REGULATIONS UNDER PUBLIC HEALTH (REGULATIONS AS TO FOOD) ACT, 1907. For some years past there has been a growing necessity for arming Port Sanitary and Local Authorities with increased powers for the prevention of distribution of imported food of a doubtful character. The public feeling aroused by the scandals created by the attempted sale of unwholesome tinned food, which formed part of the Army stores returned to this country from South Africa, together with the revelations of the manner in which the trade in meat products was conducted in Chicago, and the representations upon the subject made to the Local Government Board by the Corporation and other authorities responsible for the protection of the public health, resulted in the President of the Local Government Board pledging the Government to take action for dealing with the evil. * I regret to say Mr. Down died on 11th February last while this Report was in the Press, 126 A measure for this purpose, entitled the "Public Health (Regulations as to Food) Bill," was consequently introduced in the Commons by the President of the Local Government Board in July, 1906, but was not then proceeded with owing to the heavy pressure of Government business. It was re-introduced in February, 1907, and received the Royal Assent on the 28th August of that year. The Public Health (Regulations as to Food) Act, 1907, became immediately operative, but it was not until the 12th September, 1908, that the Local Government Board issued the Regulations made by them for carrying out the provisions of the Act, together with a Memorandum thereon. Two sets of Regulations were made, viz.: — I.—"The Public Health (First Series : Unsound Food) Regulations, 1908," and II.—"The Public Health (Foreign Meat) Regulations, 1908." These Regulations deal, with few exceptions, with all the questions relating to control of food supplies upon which representations have been made to the Local Government Board from time to time by the Corporation. I.—The Public Health (First Series; Unsound Food) Regulations, 1908. These became operative on the 1st October, 1908. They have been framed with the object of securing that articles of food which are unsound, unwholesome, or unfit for human consumption, and which, as part of the cargo of a ship, are brought to a place in this country either as a place of destination or as a place of deposit for transmission to a place of destination elsewhere in the United Kingdom, shall be dealt with at the port of discharge. This procedure is necessary for the prevention of danger to the public health from the consumption of unsound or unwholesome food. Inspection of articles of food for this purpose has for many years been carried out with satisfactory results by the Port Sanitary Authority in the Port of London, and more recently also in the Port of Manchester. The experience gained in these Ports has been utilised by the Board in preparing these Regulations, which enable action to be taken with regard to unsound food in all Port Sanitary districts and in all Municipal Boroughs and Urban and Rural districts which include or abut on any part of a Customs Port, which part is not within the jurisdiction of a Port Sanitary Authority. It should be noted that the main feature of these Regulations is the exercise of control over food stuffs from abroad at the port of entry, and that the Local Government Board have not only power to make Regulations for this purpose, but may designate the authority for their administration. In those now under consideration the several Port Sanitary Authorities, in conjunction with the Customs, are detailed for the purpose, but the Local Government Board may direct by Order that a Port Sanitary Authority shall act with the Local Sanitary Authority when they consider joint action to be necessary. 127 II.—The Public Health (Foreign Meat) Regulations, 1908. These came into operation on 1st January, 1909. They are designed to protect the public from the danger entailed from the unrestricted importation of certain kinds of meat foods, which are classified as — Foreign Meat of Class I. „ „ „ II „ „ „ III. " Foreign Meat of Class I." means foreign meat in the form:— (a) Of scrap meat, namely, meat which, whether it is fresh, or has, before importation, been subjected to a process of freezing or other refrigeration, or to chemical or other treatment, with or without the addition of any preservative or colouring substance— (i.) Consists of scraps, trimmings or other pieces of such shape or in such condition as to afford insufficient means of identification with definite parts of a carcass ; (ii.) Has not, before importation, been made ready for human consumption in the form of a sausage, or of other prepared or manufactured articles of food ; and (iii.) Is without bone in its natural state of attachment ; or (b) Of tripe, namely, any edible part of the stomach, or of a tongue or kidney, to which formalin, or a solution, or other preparation of, or comprising formic aldehyde; or a compound containing fluorine or boron; or salicylic acid, formic acid, sulphurous acid, benzoic acid, or any compound or any such acid has been applied. (c) Of several parts of the carcass of a pig, or of other edible parts of a pig, which have not, before importation, been salted, cured, pickled, dried or smoked, or otherwise prepared as bacon or ham, and which are not contained in a box, case, receptacle or package with an official certificate impressed thereon, or affixed or attached thereto. "Foreign Meat of Class II.'' means:— Foreign meat which, being in the form of the entire carcass of a pig, has not, before importation, been salted, cured, pickled, dried or smoked, or otherwise prepared as bacon or ham, and is without the head in its natural state of attachment to the carcass, and is without the lymphatic glands about the throat, and any other part of the carcass in their natural position. "Foreign Meat of Class III." means:— Foreign meat which is in the form of several parts of the carcass of a pig, or of other edible parts of a pig, which has not before importation been salted, cured, pickled, dried or smoked, or otherwise prepared as 128 bacon or ham, which is not and does not comprise any foreign meat of Class I., and which is contained in a box, case, receptacle or package with an official certificate impressed thereon, or affixed or attached thereto. "Foreign Meat Unclassed" means:— Foreign meat other than foreign meat of Class I., foreign meat of Class II., or foreign meat of Class III. The Regulations provide for the manner in which meat in the several classes is to be detained by the Customs pending examination by the Medical Officer of Health. Rules are also given for the guidance of that Officer. Regulations are also made with regard to (i.) giving notice by the Sanitary Authority to the importers of their intentions with regard to consignments ; (ii.) destruction of foreign meat; (iii.) proceedings for proof that foreign meat is not intended for sale; (iv.) prohibition of removal for sale of foreign meat in certain cases, and other matters of administrative detail in connection with the subject. The Regulations will meet the case of the imported "boxed " or barrelled meat, which it has been my duty to so strongly object to under former conditions of importation. A Report of the Sanitary Committee, agreed to by the Court of Common Council on the 31st October, 1907, contained a recommendation of your Medical Officer of Health that the attention of the Local Government Board should be directed to the importance of making Regulations under the Act for the following purpose:— "For preventing the admission of any meat, or meat products, into this "country that have not been officially examined and certified as whole"some before export, the inspection to be in accord with the standard in "force in this country at the time. At present the only standard "existing is that laid down in the recommendations of the Royal "Commission on Tuberculosis, 1898." It will be seen from the following definition in the Foreign Meat Regulations that, with regard to this point, the Local Government Board requisitions entirely comply with the recommendations of the Corporation:— "The expression ' Official Certificate' used in relation to foreign meat "or in relation to a box, case, receptacle, or package containing foreign "meat, means a certificate, label, mark, stamp or other voucher that the "cattle or pig from which the meat is derived has been certified by a "competent authority in the place of origin to be free from disease at "the time of slaughter, and that the meat has been certified by the like "authority to have been dressed or prepared, and packed with the needful "observance of all requirements for the prevention of danger arising to "public health from the meat as an article of food." In a Report dated the 27 th October, 1908, it was pointed out by your Medical Officer that the administration of both sets of these Regulations was 129 placed in the hands of the Port Sanitary Authority, and that it would be in the best interests of the public, merchants, and others concerned, for the Local Government Board to issue orders, under Articles XVII. and XII. of the Regulations respectively, requiring joint action by the Common Council and the Port Sanitary Authority of the Port of London for the purposes of the Regulations in the matter. The Board concurred therein, and on the 4th December issued for consideration of the Court of Common Council draft Regulations to be applied to Orders made by them with respect to each of the above-mentioned sets of Regulations. These drafts were approved by the Court of Common Council on the 10th December, and the Local Government Board notified accordingly. Orders for the purpose indicated were issued by the Local Government Board on the 29th December last, and came into operation on the 1st January of this year. They may be cited as— "The City of London (First Series, Unsound Food Regulations) Order, 1908," and "The City of London (Foreign Meat Regulations) Order, 1908" respectively. The effect of the these Orders will be that in the case of the Public Health (First Series) Unsound Food Regulations the powers and duties under them in regard to food landed in the City—except in the cases referred to in paragraph 2 of the Order—will be exercised by the Common Council and their Medical Officer of Health. In cases where the Port Medical Officer has specified a place in the City for the detention of food, he is to notify the Medical Officer of Health of the City, who will thereupon deal with the food. When food consigned to a wharf in the City has been examined by the Port Medical Officer and found to be in good condition, he will, if the importer desires it, notify the Medical Officer of Health of the City of the fact of its examination, and no further examination will then be made by that Medical Officer of Health unless he has reason to think that the food has subsequently become unsound. With regard to the Order respecting Foreign Meat Regulation it is proposed that meat which, after it has been landed in the City, is made the subject of notice by the Officer of Customs, the Council and Medical Officer—except in the cases specified in paragraph 3—shall deal with it. If the Port Medical Officer has given a Certificate releasing any foreign meat from detention, under sub-division (5) of Article III of the Regulations, no further examination will then be made by the Medical Officer of Health of the City unless he has reason to believe that the meat has subsequently become unsound. 130 Orders have been issued relating to similar joint action on the part of the Port Sanitary Authority with the following Metropolitan Borough Councils, viz. : Stepney, Berinondsey, and Poplar. The assistance of the staff of Meat Inspectors being indispensable for carrying out the additional obligations imposed upon the Corporation by these Orders, the Chief Inspector of Slaughterhouses and Meat, and six of the Inspectors of Slaughterhouses and Meat were, on the 4th February of this year, subject to the approval of the Local Government Board, appointed Assistant Officers under Article XV of the Foreign Meat Regulations and Article XI of the Unsound Food Regulations, such appointments being duly confirmed by the Local Government Board on the 3rd March, 1909. It being evident that Steamship and Railway Companies engaged in the transport of Foreign Imported Meat were greatly interested in these Regulations, your Medical Officer, with a view to facilitate their working, convened a conference at the Guildhall on the 22nd December last, which was attended by representatives of the following Steamship and Railway Companies:— The General Steam Navigation Co. The John Cockerill Line. W. H. Muller and Co. (the Batavier Line). Phillips and Graves (representing the Holland Steamship Co.*). The Great Eastern Railway Co. South Eastern and Chatham Railway Co. (representing the Zeeland Shipping Co.). Of the above companies five, viz., the General Steam Navigation Co., the Batavier, the Holland Steamship Co., the Great Eastern Railway Co., and the South Eastern and Chatham Railway Co. (for the Zeeland Shipping Co.), convey practically the whole of the meat exported to London from Holland. The John Cockerill Line carry almost solely from Belgium. PIGS. With the exception of the South Eastern and Chatham Railway Co., on behalf of the Zeeland Shipping Co., all the shippers at present engaged in the Dutch trade agreed to your Medical Officer's suggestion that the best and simplest method of complying with the Regulations, so far as pigs from Holland were concerned, would be (1) For all carriers to refuse absolutely to ship on board their boats the carcasses of pigs that did not bear the Netherlands Government label indicating that such carcasses had been subjected to official inspection and found vyholesome. * This Company are now represented by the British Amsterdam Maritime Agency. 131 (2) To give notice to this Authority, at the earliest possible moment, of the time of arrival and the nature of the cargo, i.e., the quantity of pigs and other' meat on board. (3) They further undertook that all pigs should be sent direct to the Central Meat Market, except with the previous consent of your Medical Officer. The South Eastern and Chatham Railway and the Zeeland Shipping Co., being unable at that time to agree to carry only labelled carcasses, subsequently undertook that unlabelled carcasses only would be carried by their day boat from Flushing, and that the labelled ones should be dispatched by their night boat from the same port. With regard to the Belgian pigs'shipped by the John Cockerill Line, it was pointed out that as the British standard of tuberculosis is not reached in Belgium, the certificate of the Government of that country could not be accepted as a guarantee that the meat complied with the Regulations, and therefore the carcasses of such pigs would be liable to detention for inspection on arrival. As the initiative of action rests with the Officer of H.M. Customs, except in cases where the Medical Officer may desire to examine meat arriving from a specified country or port of shipment of particular consignments, arrangements were made with the Chief Officer of Customs in the City to detain (1) All carcasses of pigs from Belgium ; (2) All carcasses of pigs from Holland not bearing the official inspection label of the Netherlands Government landed at any wharves within the City limits, pending examination by myself or assistant officers. MEAT (OTHER THAN PORK). It was explained that the regulations provided means by which foreign meat generally, which is diseased, unsound, unwholesome, or unfit for human consumption can be dealt with, and such meat is therefore liable to detention for the purpose of examination. SCRAP MEAT, &c. With regard to the above, the Regulations are designed to deal with foreign butchers' meat which is imported in the form of scraps, trimmings, or other pieces not sufficiently identifiable with definite parts of a carcass; tripe, tongues, or kidneys treated with certain preservatives, or pieces of fresh pork contained in a package not bearing an "official certificate." These meats are classed together as "Foreign Meat, Class I.," and the representatives were informed that the regulations with regard thereto would be strictly enforced. 132 It was clearly understood that these arrangements were made with a view to minimising any delay that otherwise might be incurred in the execution of the duties imposed by the Regulations, and that the Corporation did not part with any of its rights or powers under them, reserving the right to check the meat from time to time at any point, and to vary and revoke them without notice. The "London Gazette," of the 22nd January last, gave notices of " official certificate" declared to be admissible in relation to Foreign Meat of Class III. admitted for import under Article III. of the Foreign Meat Regulations, which sanction the importation of fresh pork contained in a box, case, receptacle or package under certain conditions, one being that it should bear an "official certificate" indicating that it has been certified by competent authority in the place of origin to be free from disease at the time of slaughter, and that the meat has been certified by like authority to have been dressed or prepared and packed with the needful observance of all requirements for the prevention of danger arising to public health as an article of food. Such "official certificate" means a certificate, label, mark or stamp, or other voucher which, by a notice published in the " London Gazette," is declared to be admissible for a certain period. The notices in the Gazette referred to related to official certificates declared to be admissible in relation to Foreign Meat of Class III. imported from Denmark, the Netherlands, the Dominion of Canada, and the Dominion of New Zealand. The effect of these Regulations will be to practically stop the importation of a certain class of American frozen boxed meat, the undesirable nature of which has frequently been brought to your notice. The importation of tubercular pigs from the Continent will also be checked, and tripes, tongues, and kidneys saturated with compounds containing boron and other preservatives will be kept out of the country. Copies of the Regulations and Orders referred to in the foregoing are given in Appendix E, p. 186. REFRIGERATION. The great development of the system of Refrigeration, and its important bearing on the preservation of foods, was well shown by the marked success which attended the First International Congress of the Refrigerating Industry, held in Paris in October, 1908, to which your Medical Officer was appointed a British delegate. The Congress was attended by delegates from nearly every country in the world, and many useful and instructive discussions took place. 133 Perhaps the best proof of the high standard to which the trade has attained is the fact that the following resolution was unanimously passed by the Congress :— "That the refrigerating industry having attained world-wide importance, it is "highly desirable that exact scientific; data be obtained for determining the "conditions as to time and temperature under which perishable produce can be "satisfactorily kept. And that with greatest respect to all other countries, the "Corporation of the City of London, as the largest market authority, be asked to "conduct these inquiries." Hitherto it has been commonly believed that the freezing of meat properly carried out will ensure its preservation for an indefinite period, disregarding altogether the fact, now well established, that there is a distinct class of bacteria which live and flourish at the low temperatures of the freezing chamber. While on the one hand it is impossible to exaggerate the value of the freezing system in preserving food for long periods in a thoroughly wholesome and nutritious condition, it must always be borne in mind that there is a limit to its value, and that this limit will vary with the nature and condition of the food so treated. The working out of this important question will be of the utmost value both to the trade and the consumer. A complete investigation of the various problems involved has been undertaken by the United States Department of Agriculture. * In a recent report dealing with frozen chickens, issued by Dr. H. W. Wiley, it is stated that— "Macroscopic observations of fresh and cold stored chickens show that certain "plainly visible differences exist between the two classes, which differences are "progressive, depending on the length of the storage period," and that " in the "case of frozen birds there is no indication of any improvement in quality, that is, "in taste, odour, or flavour, during cold storage. There is a deterioration which is "noticeable, even at the end of three months, and becomes more marked as the "time of storage grows longer. Hence, without any reference whatever to the "question of wholesomeness, cold storage prolonged for six months or more "appears to be distinctly detrimental as far as taste, flavour, and palatability are "concerned."
