Of the twelve Danish samples, two only contained preservatives in extensive quantities. The representatives of the American firms protested against the condemnation of their goods on the ground that the quantity of preservative found was small, but were not prepared to dispute the matter before a magistrate. Instructions were at once cabled to the American packers to discontinue sending their goods to the London market. The consignors had a legitimate grievance, inasmuch as there is no fixed legal limit as to the nature and amount of preservative permissible, but this question will not arise again, as the importation of meat of this class is now prohibited by the Public Health (Foreign Meat) Regulations now in force. The question of boric acid in sausages is now sub judice, notice of appeal having been lodged with regard to a conviction in a case of this kind at a Metropolitan Police Court. MARKING OF MEAT AT THE CENTRAL MARKETS. In my Annual Report for 1907 reference was made to the suggestion that the time was opportune for the Corporation to offer facilities for marking meat that could be brought to a depot established for the purpose at the Central Markets. The system would be a voluntary one, and a nominal charge made for inspection and marking which might cover the expense incurred. In April last year an opportunity appeared to present itself for initiating a system of this kind. It arose in consequence of the receipt of an application from a firm carrying on an extensive business as Preserved Provision Manufacturers for certificates of official inspection of meat purchased by them in the City Markets. This was necessary, as the firm in question did considerable trade with the United States of America, in which country such certificates were required under Regulations made under Section II of the Pure Food Law, 1906, of the U.S.A. The facts being reported to the Committee, it was left to the Medical Officer to take the necessary steps for making the necessary inspection and the issue of certificates. The following arrangements were then made for this purpose. The Chief Inspector of Meat was instructed to arrange as to the method of inspection, which could easily be carried out at the shops at Smithfield when the meat is purchased, in order to avoid any unnecessary delay or trouble. 136 That a label signed by the Inspector would be attached by wire to each piece of meat by an impressed leaden seal, bearing the City Arms on one side and P.H.D. (Public Health Department) on the other. The labels were to be numbered consecutively, and a record kept at the Market of meat so examined and passed. A certificate would be forwarded to the purchaser each day, showing a copy of this record, stating that the consecutive numbers correspond to those on the labels. It was proposed that in the first instance a charge of two pence be made for each seal so attached, in order to cover the cost of marking, and that this charge be reconsidered later. It was explained that this charge was solely for the extra work and expense involved in the marking, and not for inspection to which they were entitled. These arrangements were to have been carried out for a limited period— say, three or six months—in order to ascertain whether the system worked satisfactory. The Committee concurred herein, and decided that, in the meantime, the whole question of marking meat be adjourned, pending the result of the working of the proposed arrangement. This, however, never came into operation owing to the Local Government Board issuing Regulations with regard to the exportation of meat food products to the United States of America and the Philippine Islands, referred to on page 138 of this Report. With regard to this question of meat marking, it was found that in this case the firm welcomed the proposed method, as their interests were concerned, and the point I here wish to emphasise, and what I have before pointed out, is that the interests of British Agriculturalists are equally concerned in the matter, as there is little doubt that a great deal of foreign meat is sold as home produce, and this view has been endorsed by the Cattle Markets Committee. The fact is patent that anyone purchasing meat would prefer to know that it had been subjected to official inspection by experts, and failing the substitution of Public Abattoirs for private slaughterhouses, the official marking of meat that has passed the inspectors is the only means by which the public can have any choice in the matter. It is obvious that of two butchers established in the same neighbourhood, one selling unmarked meat and the other officially marked meat, the one selling the latter would get most of the business. . If the Corporation make provision for the examination and marking of meat at a dep6t established at Smithfield, as suggested in the Report presented to 137 the Sanitary Committee on the 22nd October, 1907, it would remain to be seen how far the salesmen would make use of it, but at any rate I am of opinion they should certainly have the opportunity of availing themselves of such a system. It must be remembered that it would be more in the interest of the salesman to have a voluntary system of the kind established, and for him to make use of it, than that he should be subjected to compulsory Regulations made by the Local Government Board for a similar purpose. It is true that up to the present the Board have only dealt with imported food and meat, but their powers under the Public Health (Regulations as to Food) Act are practically unlimited. The practice of marking inspected meat is common in other countries, and in view of the increased interest now taken by the public with regard to the wholesomeness or quality of foodstuffs generally, it is more than likely that the practice will be extended to this country. The Corporation has ever been in the van in all movements in connection with the public welfare, and have modelled their administration of sanitary affairs with a view of ensuring that in all respects it should be in harmony with the spirit of the age. The traditions of the office, therefore, I have the honour to hold, impel me to again direct attention to this subject, and I would call attention to the fact that any owner of carcasses slaughtered at Islington Cattle Market can have his meat labelled after inspection, and that this system has been taken advantage of by some large dealers. EXPORT OF MEAT FOOD PRODUCTS TO CERTAIN FOREIGN COUNTRIES. To Argentina. In November, 1907, the Local Government Board issued a memorandum for the guidance of persons engaged in the export of meat products to Argentina. In consequence of a decree issued on the 4th October, 1906, by the Argentine Government, requiring that all pigs and other imported animal products should be accompanied by a certificate (duly legalised by an Argentine Consul) of the country where they were manufactured, to the effect that the factory where the goods were prepared was under sanitary supervision, the Local Government Board, after consultation with the Board of Agriculture and the Board of Trade, formulated certain conditions, duly set forth in the memorandum, under which meat products may be accompanied by an official certificate which the Argentine Government agreed to accept with respect to meat products exported from Great Britain. The official certificate can only be issued by an official designated to act as " certifying officer " duly appointed by the Local Government Board. 138 Your Medical Officer's appointment as certifying officer was sanctioned by the Board on 6th November, 1907. The certifying officer is required to keep himself informed, by personal inquiry and other available ways, as to — 1. Structure and character of premises on which the meat foods are prepared, stored, or packed (as the case may be) in regard to opportunities of objectionable or unwholesome contamination of these foods, at any stage of their preparation, storage, or packing. 2. Cleanliness and wholesomeness of methods employed in preparing, storing, or packing the meat foods in question. 3. Precautions taken to avoid the utilisation for food purposes of diseased, unsound, or unwholesome meat in any portion of the premises; and arrangements safely to dispose of any meat which, on account of disease, unsoundness, unwholesomeness, or for any other reason is unfit for human food. If the information obtained by the certifying officer in regard to (1), (2) and (3) above is not in his view satisfactory, certification is to be withheld. Information is forthwith to be given to the Board by the certifying officer in any instance where he has refused to grant a certificate. The certifying officer will, as and when requested by the Board, give such information as may be required by them in regard to the methods of inquiry, inspection, and certification which have been adopted in regard to the premises in question. If the Board have reason to believe that improper use is being made at any time of any declaration, or of any certificate given by a certifying officer respecting any premises, the Board will cease to authorise the issue of official certificates in respect of such premises. To the United States of America and the Philippine Islands. Very similar procedure to the foregoing is now required in the case of meat food products exported to the United States of America and the Philippine Islands. In consequence of the Food Laws in force in these places, requiring that all imported meat food products shall be accompanied by an official attestation of precautions taken to safeguard the wholesomeness of the foods in question, the Local Government Board, at the request of the Foreign Office, have issued a memorandum for the guidance of persons in England and Wales engaged in the export of such articles to these places. The Regulations laid down were almost precisely the same as those relating to meat products exported to the Argentine, the only difference being that in addition to the certifying officer being required to keep himself informed with 139 regard to paragraphs marked 1, 2 and 3 on page 138, he has been charged with the additional duty of noting the 4. Steps taken to ensure that all meat foods prepared or packed for export to the United States of America and the Philippine Islands are solely the product of animals which, on adequate ante-mortem and post-mortem inspection, have been found free from disease and suitable for human food; and if this is not complied with he must withhold the certificate. Your Medical Officer was also appointed certifying officer in this case. It should be noted that certificates for Argentina have to be vised by an Argentine Consul, while the United States Government recognises the authority of certifying officers without the formality of viseing. Copies of the Scheduled Forms of Declarations and Certificates required in both cases are given in Appendix F, page 207. INSPECTION OF NEW ZEALAND MEAT. In February, 1908, with the concurrence of the Sanitary Committee, the Assistant Chief Veterinarian, New Zealand, at the request of the Chief Veterinarian of the Government of that country, attended at the Central Markets for the purpose of obtaining full information as to the inspection there of meat imported from New Zealand and the standard adopted in dealing with it. In March. 1908, Mr. Alexander Crabbe, M.R.C.V.S., Glasgow, was appointed by the New Zealand Government as their permanent Officer, with instructions to make himself acquainted with all matters relating to New Zealand meat in this country, and especially as to the sufficiency or otherwise of the inspection in the country of production. It is hoped this course will prove beneficial both to the exporters and the dealers in this country, as it will lead to combined action in both countries. INSPECTION OF KOSHER MEAT. In May last, in consequence of some question as to the soundness of some meat passed by the Jewish Inspectors, the matter was brought to the notice of the Board of Shecheta, who were most anxious to co-operate in order to prevent such occurrences in future. The Board, therefore, at once made arrangements for their Killers and Inspectors to attend a course of lectures on meat inspection and be instructed by practical demonstration and the examination of carcasses of oxen, sheep and calves intended for human consumption, certificates being granted to those satisfying the examiner of their competency. This was a most satisfactory conclusion of a difficult question, and the Board of Shecheta were informed of the Corporation's cordial approval of their action. 140 IMPORTATION OF IMMATURE CALVES. In May last, the attention of the Netherlands Government was directed to the impropriety of their Inspectors passing as fit for export to this country calves of such an exceedingly low weight as to leave room for doubt as to whether the animal had any independent existence. On two days in March, four calves, weighing respectively 34 lbs., 27 lbs., 37 lbs. and 35 lbs., were seized in the Central Meat Market. Such puny animals by reason of their miniature condition are certainly not fit for food. In the City, the sale of calves for food, weighing less than 48 lbs. or of a less age than three weeks, is prohibited. REGULATIONS FOR MEAT INTENDED FOR EXPORT FROM THE NETHERLANDS. In September last, I was favoured by the Consul-General for the Netherlands with an English translation of the Netherlands Decree of June 24th, 1908, Number 50, prescribing the Regulations for carrying into effect Section 5 of the Netherlands Inspection of Meat for Export Law, 1907. Hitherto, the inspection and marking of meat for export from Holland has been conducted upon voluntary lines, a method that has proved most useful in preparing the way for a compulsory system. The following summary of these Regulations shows that the Netherlands Government is determined that, so far as statutory requirements are concerned, that an endeavour to ensure that meat for export from Holland shall be above suspicion. These deal with:— I. The manner of slaughtering animals, the meat of which is subject to inspection before export. II. The communes and places where, and the times at which, meat can be submitted for inspection before export. III. The state in which meat shall be submitted for inspection or transport before export. IV. The rules for the inspection of meat intended for export, the standard to be adopted for such meat, and the mode of dealing with it by sterilization or destruction when rejected for export. V. The manner in which the meat intended for export' is marked, and the arrangement of the marks. 141 These regulations will prohibit the passing of meat for export in the following circumstances:— If it is the meat of animals that have not been completely inspected. If on inspection any of the diseases referred to in a Schedule are found to exist. In cases of Tuberculosis or other nature, if such meat is condemned, in the country of destination. In cases of bone fractures and important lesions or wounds of the skin and subcutis. When in pigs extensive skin affections are found to exist. If local processes or abscesses have been removed before inspection.' When the meat has not been handled in a cleanly manner. In all cases in which serious doubt exists as to the fitness for export. With regard to Tuberculosis, these regulations will have the effect of preventing the export to this country of pork showing this disease "in any degree," inasmuch as the standard laid down by the Royal Commission on Tuberculosis, 1898, will be adopted by Inspecting Officers in the Netherlands. Unfortunately, there has been some delay in fixing the date when these regulations become operative. The 1st January of this year was the original date fixed, but this has been postponed. In the meantime, the Local Government Board have agreed to accept, until the 1st May, 1909, the labels scheduled in these regulations as evidence of proper inspection, and the same have been gazetted as "Official Certificates " inaccordance with the requirements of the Public Health (Foreign Meat) Regulations, 1908. When these Regulations come into force in the Netherlands it will mark a great step in the system of Meat Inspection, and if carried out on the lines laid down it will be of immense advantage to the trade of that country and to the public here to have meat supplied with a guarantee of its freedom from disease. At the same time it must not be forgotten that this meat is sold in competition with the British and Colonial product, and that if this trade rivalry is to be on a fair basis, the British farmer should, in his own interests, insist upon the inspection and marking of his home-killed animals. In other words, the public Abbatoir system is the only one which will sufficiently protect, at the same time, the British producer and the public health. 142 QUALIFICATIONS OF MEAT AND FOOD INSPECTORS. In April last, I directed the attention of the Sanitary Committee to the conditions prevailing with regard to the appointment of these Officers. Under the Public Health Acts, a Food Inspector must possess a Sanitary Inspector's Certificate, however well qualified he may be for the position in other respects. In cases where an Officer's whole time is taken up with Food Inspection, most of the subjects in which he has to quality in order to gain such certificate are practically of no use to him in the performance of his duties, and he has, therefore, been put to much useless waste of time and expense when he would have been better occupied in acquiring a wider knowledge of matters relating to foodstuffs. At the present day, there is a general trend of public opinion in the direction of securing a more complete supervision of food supplies, and, as a consequence, there is an increasing demand for the services of men possessing special qualifications for the office of Food Inspector. Meat inspection, for instance, can only be satisfactorily performed by men who possess a practical knowledge of the butchers' trade, and the Corporation have made it a rule since the year 1861, in making such appointments, to act on this principle. It is clearly an anomaly that, before men with special experience, and therefore peculiarly well qualified for the office, can be appointed, they should be compelled to pass an examination embracing nearly every conceivable subject connected with Sanitary science, which is subsequently almost valueless to them in the execution of their duties. In the interests of both the public and the trader, it is essential that Food Inspectors should have undergone a thorough practical training in foodstuffs, and possess such special qualifications as the Local Government Board may approve. 143 Table showing the average deliveries at the Central Meat Market, and other details for successive quinquennial periods during the past 35 years:— Quinquennium. As the result of the daily inspection of the City wharves, 153 loads of unsound food were removed from these places during the year by the Cleansing Department. The material so disposed of comprised:— Tons. Cwts. Qrs. Tinned fruit and vegetables 51 2 0 Condensed milk 20 11 2 Miscellaneous tinned food 29 18 2 Fruit pulp 27 3 0 Fruit and vegetables 39 15 0 Miscellaneous food 2 19 1 Total 171 9 1 The greater portion of the above was removed at the written request of the owners. Section 47 (8) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, provides that— "Where a person has in his possession any article which is unsound "or unwholesome or unfit for the food of man, he may, by written "notice to the Sanitary Authority, specifying such article, and containing "a sufficient identification of it, request its removal, and the Sanitary "Authority shall cause it to be removed as if it were trade refuse." This provision takes away the ground of a common defence in prosecutions for being in possession of unsound food. It has often been suggested that the food was set aside with the intention of destroying it before it was discovered by the Inspector. This power to notify to Sanitary Authorities under this section affords a test where this line of defence is adopted. Additional powers with regard to dealing with imported food have now been conferred upon your Officers under the City of London (First Series, Unsound Food Regulations) Order, 1908, made under the provisions of the Public Health (Regulations as to Food) Act, 1907, referred to at length on p. 125. 150 SALE OF FOOD AND DRUGS ACTS. During the year 1,183 articles of food and drugs were analysed under the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts, 1875 to 1899. Of these, 78, or 5.67 per cent., were certified by the Public Analyst to be adulterated*, or not up to standard, as compared with 6.68 per cent. in 1907. It would appear from Table I. on p. 150, that the percentage of adulterated samples remained at practically the same figure as last year, but if the samples of meat products referred to on p. 133 are omitted—these having been specially selected in relation to the question of preservatives in such 152 articles imported from abroad—the percentage of adulterated articles found was 5.11 per cent., being a satisfactory decrease from the corresponding figures of previous years. As on other occasions, both your Public Analyst and myself have commented during the year on the unsatisfactory situation created by the unsettled state of affairs with regard to the presence of preservatives in foodstuffs. In reporting on 24th March, 1908, upon two samples of kidneys containing excessive quantities of boric acid, it was pointed out that a man making a reasonable meal of such treated material would ingest in the one case one-and-two-thirds, and in the other two-and-a-half times as much as the maximum dose of boric acid when used medicinally. Further, there is no justification for the presence of such quantities of preservatives, and there are special objections to their presence at all, as the food may be in a state of decomposition before treatment, the evidence of which would be masked by the drug used. One element in this question, viz. the situation both of the trader and the Public Analyst owing to the absence of any regulation on the subject, is put very clearly by your Public Analyst in his Report for the third Quarter of 1908, thus— "Whether salt, saltpetre, boric acid or sulphurous acid should be "permitted to be used are all fair questions for investigation and settle"ment, but such settlement should be by Act of Parliament, and not by "the tedious process of prosecuting vendors for having these or other "preservatives present, and moreover it seems scarcely fair to bring a "criminal charge against a vendor merely to settle a scientific question. "The Board of Agriculture have had statutory powers since 1899 to "limit or prohibit preservatives in dairy produce, and the Local Govern"ment Board have, under the Act of 1907, power to limit or prohibit "the use of any preservative in butter, margarine or milk-blended butter. "These departments have not published any regulations, and "although they both have the resources of the Government behind "them, hesitate to commit themselves. "This condition of things is not only a hardship to the trader, but also "to the Public Analyst, who has to take the responsibility of passing an "article as genuine although containing preservatives, or on the other "hand of condemning an article on account of the preservatives present. "As to which course to take he is left entirely without guidance. 153 In this connection it may serve a useful purpose to quote some observations contained in a report upon boric acid in sausages, presented by me to the Sanitary Committee on 24th November last year.*— "At present there are no statutory limitations as to the amount of "preservatives that can be used without the sale of an article being to "the prejudice of the purchaser. In July, 1899, a Departmental "Committee was appointed by the Board of Agriculture to enquire into "the use of preservatives and colouring matter in the preservation and " colouration of food, and to report "(1) Whether the use of such materials or any of them for the "preservation and colouring of food in certain quantities is injurious "to health, and if so, in what proportion does their use become "injurious. "(2) To what extent and in what amount are they so used at "the present time. "This Committee made certain recommendations with regard to milk "and milk products, and the prohibition of the use of copper salts in the "so-called greening of preserved food, but made no other recommendation "with regard to the use of preservatives in food, one reason for this "omission as stated in the report being that " ' It was soon evident that the question of food preservatives had " ' not at the time of our enquiry received special attention by the . " ' medical profession.' " "This expression of opinion was given nearly eight years ago, and "whatever the concensus of opinion of that profession on the subject "may be at the present time, there can be no question that the public "have a right to know what they are buying. "Since the date of the Departmental Committee referred to, the "physiological action of this drug has been investigated by many "observers, and the evidence now available enables a juster estimate to "be formed with regard to its action on the human system. Upon a "review of all facts, there can be no doubt that when this or any "other drug is added to a food product the purchaser should be notified "of its presence." It will be seen from the foregoing that there is an urgent demand for the settlement of the question of preservatives in food on the part of Public Health Officers, Public Analysts, Wholesale and Retail traders, Foreign importers, and * For results of Analyses, see p. 134. 154 in fact, from all concerned in the food supplies of the people. It is full time for a further inquiry into the use of preservatives and colouring matter in food, and that this question, together with the present unsatisfactory warranty section of the Food and Drugs Acts, should receive a more sympathetic consideration by the Government of the day than has hitherto obtained of the many representations on these subjects made to them by those responsible for the working of the Acts. MILK. Milk, which so readily lends itself to sophistication, has as heretofore received the special attention of your sampling officers, and comprised no less than 39 per cent. of the articles examined during the year. It is satisfactory to note there has been a marked decrease in milk adulteration in the City during 1908, as will be shown in the accompanying Table:— Year. No. of Samples Examined. Found Genuine. Found Adulterated. Percentage of Adulterated Samples. 1901 392 209 83 21.2 1902 599 519 80 13.3 1903 517 457 60 11.6 1904 452 417 35 7.7 1905 481 445 36 7.5 1906 556 513 43 7.7 1907 472 432 40 8.4 1908 469 440 29 6.2 A decrease in milk adulteration in eight years from 21.2 to 6.2 per cent. shows that an energetic and systematic administration of the Acts is greatly in the interests of the citizens, the costs of administration being more than met by the enhanced value of the food commodities sold to them. Of the 29 samples of milk reported against by the Public Analyst, the vendors of 18 were proceeded against, and in six of these, or 33⅓ per cent., the cases were dismissed upon proof of warranty. The offence in 8 cases consisted in adding water, in 9 of the removal of cream, and one sample was both skimmed and watered. The penalties imposed upon offenders on conviction varied from 1s. and 18s. 6d. expenses to £5 and 18s. 0d. expenses. That over 6 per cent. of the milk samples taken during 1908 were adulterated—in spite of the fact that during the past eight years no less than 241 prosecutions have been instituted against milk dealers alone in the City— is an indication that milk sophistication is a paying business on the whole, and unless offenders are more heavily dealt with, the evil is likely to continue. Your Public Analyst reports that milk which used to be generally mixed with preservatives was in all cases last year free from boric acid and formalin. BUTTER. Butter and Margarine Act, 1907. This Act, a full abstract of which was given on p. 148 of my Annual Report for last year, came into operation on the 1st January, 1908. Section I. (1) of the Act requires that butter factories and any premises on which milk-blended butter is manufactured, or in which the business of a wholesale dealer in milk-blended Butter is carried on, should be registered with the local authority in such manner as the Local Government Board might direct. The following are the requirements for the purpose of such registration, issued by the Local Government Board on the 28th December, 1907:— (a) The name and address of the owner or occupier making the application. (b) The situation of the factory or of the premises on which the manufacture or the business of a wholesale dealer is carried on. (c) The name and address, or the names and addresses, of the owner or owners, or occupier or occupiers, or wholesale dealer, or dealers, carrying on the manufacture or the business. (d) Whether the premises are proposed to be registered as (i.) A butter factory. (ii.) Premises on which there is manufactured any milk-blended butter, or (iii.) Premises on which there is carried on the business of a wholesale dealer in milk-blended butter. During the year two butter factories in the City were registered in accordance with the requirements, and certificates of such registration issued in due course as directed by the order of the Local Government Board. The factories referred to are situated at West Smithfield, and Custom House and Wool Quays, Lower Thames Street, respectively. 157 "Milk-blended" butter can only be manufactured for sale and sold to the public under a name to be approved by the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries. In the course of the year the undermentioned names for mixtures of butter and milk have been approved by the Board:— Bradlac. Casora. Iveleat. Jensa. Brenco. Casova. Ivelene. Kingstyle. Casa. Consumo. Ivelette. Pearks' Bread mate. Casana. Fromaid. Ivelike. Pearks' Bredspred. Casmon. Iveldale. Ivelmene. Pearks' Own. Casoa. Iveldene. Ivelmore. The permissible amount of water in these mixtures is limited by Section 4 (2) of the Act to 25 per cent. A sample of "Pearks' Own" was certified by your Public Analyst to contain 23.3 per cent. of water. A sample of Pearks' "Milk-blended Butter," taken on the 16th May, 1906, before the passing of the Butter and Margarine Act, 1907, contained no less than 28.0 per cent. of water. It is noteworthy that the price per pound of the article was 1s. 2d. in each instance. Seventeen samples of margarine were examined as to their water content, but none were found to exceed the statutory limit of 16 per cent. COFFEE. Of the 12 samples examined in 1908 one was reported against as containing no less than 50 per cent. of earth nut oil. The public should be cautioned against asking for "salad oil" if they want olive oil, as much of the former contains cotton seed or earth nut oil. Similar care should be taken when purchasing sardines. "Sardines in oil" may mean "sardines in cotton seed oil," and such is generally the case. PRESCRIPTIONS. I had occasion last year to comment on the laxity that appeared to prevail in the compounding of prescriptions, as 12 out of 25, or 52.0 per cent., made up by chemists in the City were inaccurately dispensed. During the year under review 18 were examined by your Public Analyst, and 2, or 11.1 per cent., reported against. Proceedings were instituted in each case. In one, where a chemist had supplied "Pil. plumbi cum opio" 16.9 per cent. deficient in lead acetate, the summons was dismissed owing to the form in which the Analyst's certificate was made out. The other instance was a particularly bad one as the vendor, instead of dispensing the prescription viz., Pil. plumbi cum opio, a British Pharmacopoeia preparation, supplied an article known as Plummer's Pill, to which he stated that he had added approximately three-quarters of a grain of opium, an amount considerably in excess of that which should have been present in the pill asked for, and for which he had no authority. The vendor was fined £10, and £4 4s. expenses, and as he had been convicted for offences under the Food and Drugs Act on three previous occasions, the facts were brought to the notice of both the Pharmaceutical Society and the Society of Apothecaries of London. MALT VINEGAR. Of sixteen samples of malt vinegar submitted to the Public Analyst, six, or 37.5 per cent., were certified to be "vinegar other than malt vinegar." Five prosecutions were instituted against the vendors, one case being dismissed, and in the others, fines were imposed, varying from £5 with 17s. 6d. expenses, and £8 and 18s. 6d. expenses. The result of this exposure has been that anyone asking for "malt vinegar" at small shops in the City where vinegar is sold on draught is told either that they do not keep it or that they cannot guarantee it to be made from malt. Much of the article sold as vinegar at such places is a solution of coloured and flavoured acetic acid. There is at present no statutory definition of "vinegar." There is no objection to such artificial vinegar being sold under some designation which retains the word "vinegar," provided it also makes the nature of the product apparent. In January, 1908, a most interesting report "on the preparation and sale of vinegar in relation to the Sale of Food and Drugs Act," by Dr. J. M. Hamill, was published by the Local Government Board (Medical Department), an abstract of which was presented to the Sanitary Committee by your Medical Officer on 26th May, 1908. This deals very fully with the matter, and should be consulted by anyone interested in the vinegar question. 160 161 SALE OF FOOD AND DRUGS ACTS, 1875-1907. First Quarter: Fines 24 0 0 Expenses 6 3 6 Second Quarter „ 35 2 6 ,, 9 16 6 Third Quarter „ 7 11 0 ,, 3 6 0 Fourth Quarter „ 15 0 0 ,, 5 5 6 £81 13 6 £24 11 6 Twenty-three fines were imposed during the year, as against 26, 33 and 35 in the preceding three years, the average fine and expenses paid being £4 12s. 4d., the corresponding amounts for 1905, 1906 and 1907 being £6 15s. 0d., £3 1s. 10d. and £6 15s. 0d. respectively. By direction of the Sanitary Committee twelve persons were cautioned as to the questionable or unsatisfactory character of articles sold by them. MEDICAL EXAMINATIONS. In accordance with the Standing Order of the Court that no person shall enter the employ of the Corporation or any of its Committees without previously passing a medical examination to the satisfaction of the Committee having cognizance of the appointment, 89 persons were so examined during 1908, and of these 8, or 8.98 per cent., were reported by me as physically unfit for office. 16 officers were also medically examined with a view to their retirement, and of these 13, or 81.25 per cent., were reported to be unfit for duty. In addition to the foregoing, four medical examinations were made of persons accepted by the Central (Unemployed) Body, on the recommendation of the City of London Distress Committee, for employment on the Labour Colony at Hollesley Bay, established by the Central Body under the Unemployed Workmen Act, 1905. I have the honour to be, Gentlemen, Your obedient Servant, Public Health Department, Medical Officer of Health for the City of London. Guildhall, E.C. APPENDICES.
